}}

In 1516 the [[Ottoman Empire|Ottoman Caliphate]]'s forces [[Ottoman–Mamluk War (1516–1517)|conquered Mamluk territory]].{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=26}} Agricultural villages in  witnessed a period of relative prosperity in the 16th century but were later abandoned.  was of marginal importance to the Ottoman authorities. As a result, Ottoman presence was virtually absent and reduced to annual tax collection visits.

More Arab [[Bedouin]] tribes moved into  from  and the Hejaz during the first three centuries of Ottoman rule, including the [[Banu 'Adwan|Adwan]], the [[Bani Sakhr]] and the [[Howeitat]].{{sfn|Salibi|1998|pp=26, 27}} These tribes laid claims to different parts of the region, and with the absence of a meaningful Ottoman authority,  slid into a state of anarchy that continued until the 19th century.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=27}} This led to a short-lived occupation by the [[Wahhabism|Wahhabi]] forces (1803–1812), an ultra-orthodox Islamic movement that emerged in [[Emirate of Diriyah|Najd]] (in modern-day ).{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=30}} [[Ibrahim Pasha of |Ibrahim Pasha]], son of the [[Muhammad Ali of |governor]] of the [[ Eyalet]], [[Wahhabi War|rooted out the Wahhabis]] under the request of the Ottoman sultan by 1818.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=31}}

[[File:Z Ottoman Ten arches Amman 2.jpg|thumb|right|Ottoman [[Ten Arches Bridge]] built south of Amman in 1904, as part of the [[Hejaz railway]], which stretched across the length of  and linked [[Damascus]] with [[Medina]]]]

In 1833 Pasha turned on the Ottomans and established his rule over the Levant. His policies led to the unsuccessful [[peasants' revolt in ]] in 1834.  cities of [[As-Salt]] and Al-Karak [[Siege of Al-Karak (1834)|were destroyed]] by Pasha's forces for harboring a [[Qasim al-Ahmad|peasants' revolt leader]].  rule was [[–Ottoman War (1839–41)|forcibly ended]] in 1841, with Ottoman rule restored. Only after Pasha's campaign did the Ottoman Empire try to solidify its presence in the [[ Vilayet]], which  was part of.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=37}}

A series of tax and land reforms (''[[Tanzimat]]'') in 1864 brought some prosperity back to agriculture and to abandoned villages; the end of virtual autonomy led a backlash in other areas of .{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=37}} Muslim [[Circassians]] and [[Chechens]], fleeing [[Circassian genocide| persecution]], sought refuge in the Levant. In  and with Ottoman support, Circassians first settled in the long-abandoned vicinity of Amman in 1867 and later in the surrounding villages. The Ottoman authorities' establishment of its administration, conscription and heavy taxation policies led to revolts in the areas it controlled.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=38}} 's tribes in particular revolted during the [[Shoubak revolts|Shoubak]] (1905) and the [[Karak revolt]]s (1910), which were brutally suppressed. The construction of the [[Hejaz Railway]] in 1908—stretching across the length of  and linking [[Damascus]] with [[Medina]]—helped the population economically, as  became a stopover for pilgrims.

=== Modern era ===
{{Main|Emirate of }}

[[File:030Arab (cropped).jpg|thumb|Soldiers of the Hashemite-led [[Arab Army]] holding [[Flag of the Arab Revolt|the flag]] of the [[Arab Revolt|Great Arab Revolt]] against the [[Ottoman Empire]] in the [[Hejaz]], 1916]]

Increasing policies of [[Turkification]] and centralisation adopted by the Ottoman Empire in the wake of the 1908 [[Young Turk Revolution]] disenchanted the Arabs of the Levant, which contributed to the development of an [[Arab nationalist]] movement. These changes led to the outbreak of the 1916 [[Arab Revolt]] during [[World War I]], which ended four centuries of stagnation under Ottoman rule. The revolt was led by [[Hussein bin Ali, King of Hejaz|Sharif Hussein]] of Mecca, scion of the [[Hashemites|Hashemite family]] of the Hejaz, and his sons [[Abdullah I of |Abdullah]], [[Faisal I of |Faisal]] and [[Ali bin Hussein, King of Hejaz|Ali]]. Locally, the revolt garnered the support of the  tribes, including Bedouins, Circassians and [[ Christians|Christians]].{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=41}} The [[Allies of World War&nbsp;I]], including Britain and  whose imperial interests converged with the Arabist cause, offered support.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=40}} The revolt started on 5 June 1916 from Medina and pushed northwards until the fighting reached  in the [[Battle of Aqaba]] on 6 July 1917. The revolt reached its climax when Faisal entered Damascus in October 1918 and established an Arab-led military administration in [[Occupied Enemy Territory Administration|OETA East]], later declared as the [[Arab Kingdom of ]], both of which  was part of.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=41}} During this period, the southernmost region of the country, including [[Ma'an]] and [[Aqaba]], was also [[Occupation of Ma'an|claimed by]] the neighbouring [[Kingdom of Hejaz]].

The nascent Hashemite Kingdom over the [[ (region)|region of ]] was forced to surrender to  troops on 24 July 1920 during the [[Battle of Maysalun]]; the  occupied only the northern part of , leaving [[Interregnum ()| in a period of interregnum]]. Arab aspirations failed to gain international recognition, due mainly to the secret 1916 [[Sykes–Picot Agreement]], which divided the region into  and  spheres of influence, and the 1917 [[Balfour Declaration]], in which Britain announced its support for the establishment of a "national home" for Jews in .{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=34}} This was seen by the Hashemites and the Arabs as a betrayal of their previous agreements with the ,{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=71}} including the 1915 [[McMahon–Hussein Correspondence]], in which the  stated their willingness to recognise the independence of a unified Arab state stretching from [[Aleppo]] to [[Aden]] under the rule of the Hashemites.

[[File:The high commissioner's first visit to , in Es-Salt..jpg|thumb|[[As-Salt]] residents gather on 20 August 1920 during the [[Mandate for |]] [[High Commissioners for  and |High Commissioner]]'s visit to .]]
 [[List of high commissioners for  and |High Commissioner]] [[Herbert Samuel, 1st Viscount Samuel|Herbert Samuel]] travelled to  on 21 August 1920 to meet with As-Salt's residents. He there declared to a crowd of 600  notables that the  government would aid the establishment of local governments in , which was to be kept separate from that of . The second meeting took place in [[Umm Qais]] on 2 September, where the  representative Major [[FitzRoy Somerset, 4th Baron Raglan|Fitzroy Somerset]] received a petition that demanded: an independent Arab government in  to be led by an Arab prince (emir); land sale in  to [[Jews]] be stopped as well as the prevention of Jewish immigration there; that Britain establish and fund a national army; and that free trade be maintained between  and the rest of the region.

Abdullah, the second son of Sharif Hussein, [[Establishment of the Emirate of |arrived from Hejaz by train in Ma'an]] in southern  on 21 November 1920 to redeem the Greater  Kingdom his brother had lost.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=82}}  then was in disarray, widely considered to be ungovernable with its dysfunctional local governments.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=91}} Abdullah gained the trust of 's tribal leaders before scrambling to convince them of the benefits of an organised government.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=100}} Abdullah's successes drew the envy of the , even when it was in their interest.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=101}} The  reluctantly accepted Abdullah as ruler of  after having given him a six-month trial.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=96}} In March 1921, the  decided to add  to their [[Mandate for ]], in which they would implement their "[[Sharifian Solution]]" policy without applying the provisions of the mandate dealing with Jewish settlement. On 11 April 1921 the [[Emirate of ]] was established with Abdullah as emir.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|p=93}}

In September 1922, the Council of the [[League of Nations]] recognised  as a state under the terms of the [[ memorandum]].  remained a  mandate until 1946, but it had been granted a greater level of autonomy than the region west of the  River. Multiple difficulties emerged upon the assumption of power in the region by the Hashemite leadership.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|pp=10, 30, 31, 49, 104}} In , small local [[Kura rebellion|rebellions at Kura]] in 1921 and 1923 were suppressed by Abdullah's forces with the help of the .{{sfn|Salibi|1998|pp=10, 30, 31, 49, 104}} Wahhabis from Najd regained strength and [[Ikhwan raids on |repeatedly raided]] the southern parts of his territory, seriously threatening the emir's position.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|pp=10, 30, 31, 49, 104}} The emir was unable to repel those raids without the aid of the local Bedouin tribes and the , who maintained a military base with a small [[Royal Air Force]] detachment close to Amman.{{sfn|Salibi|1998|pp=10, 30, 31, 49, 104}}

=== Post-independence ===
{{Main|Timeline of the Hashemite Kingdom of }}

[[File:King Abdullah I of  declaring independence, 25 May 1946.jpg|thumb|right|[[Abdullah I of |King Abdullah I]] on 25 May 1946 declaring independence, with Prime Minister [[Ibrahim Hashem]] in the background]]

The [[Treaty of London (1946)|Treaty of London]], signed by the  government and the Emir of  on 22 March 1946, recognised the independence of the state. On 25 May 1946, the day that the treaty was ratified by the  parliament,  was raised to the status of a kingdom under the name of the ''Hashemite Kingdom of '' in Arabic, with Abdullah as its first king; although it continued to be referred to as the Hashemite Kingdom of  in  until 1949. 25 May is now celebrated as the nation's [[Independence Day ()|Independence Day]], a [[Public holidays in |public holiday]].  became a member of the United Nations on 14 December 1955.

On 15 May 1948, as part of the [[1948  war]],  intervened along with several other Arab states. Following the war,  controlled the [[West Bank]], and on 24 April 1950  formally [[ annexation of the West Bank|annexed these territories]] after the [[Jericho Conference]]. In response, some Arab countries demanded 's expulsion from the [[Arab League]]. On 12 June 1950, the Arab League declared that the annexation was a temporary, practical measure and that  was holding the territory as a "trustee" pending a future settlement.

King Abdullah was assassinated at the [[Al-Aqsa Mosque]] in 1951 by a  militant, amid rumors he intended to sign a peace treaty with . Abdullah was succeeded by his son [[Talal of |Talal]], who established the [[Constitution of |country's modern constitution]] in 1952. Illness caused Talal to abdicate to his eldest son [[Hussein of |Hussein]], who ascended to the throne in 1953 at age 17.  witnessed great political uncertainty in the following period. The 1950s was a period of political upheaval, as [[Nasserism]] and [[Pan-Arabism]] swept the Arab World. On 1 March 1956, King Hussein [[Arabization of the  Army command|Arabised the command of the Army]] by dismissing a number of senior  officers, an act made to remove remaining foreign influence in the country. In 1958,  and neighbouring [[Kingdom of |Hashemite ]] formed the [[Arab Federation]] as a response to the formation of the rival [[United Arab Republic]] between Nasser's  and . The union lasted only six months, being dissolved after  King [[Faisal II]] (Hussein's cousin) was deposed by a bloody [[14 July Revolution|military coup on 14 July 1958]].

[[File:Karama aftermath 1.jpg|thumb|right|[[Hussein of |King Hussein]] on 21 March 1968 checking an abandoned [[]]i tank in the aftermath of the [[Battle of Karameh]]]]

 signed a military pact with  just before  launched a preemptive strike on  to begin the [[Six-Day War]] in June 1967, where  and  joined the war. The Arab states were defeated, and  lost control of the West Bank to . The [[War of Attrition]] with  followed, which included the 1968 [[Battle of Karameh]] where the combined forces of the [[ Armed Forces]] and the [[ Liberation Organization| Liberation Organisation]] (PLO) repelled an  attack on the [[Karameh]] camp on the  border with the West Bank. Despite the fact that the  had limited involvement against the  forces, the events at Karameh gained wide recognition and acclaim in the Arab world. As a result, there was an upsurge of support for  paramilitary elements (the [[ fedayeen|fedayeen]]) within  from other Arab countries. The fedayeen activities soon became a threat to 's rule of law. In September 1970, the  army targeted the fedayeen and the resultant fighting led to the expulsion of  fighters from various PLO groups into , in a conflict that became known as [[Black September]].

In 1973,  and  waged the [[Yom Kippur War]] on , and fighting occurred along the 1967 [[ River]] cease-fire line.  sent a brigade to  to attack  units on  territory but did not engage  forces from  territory. At the [[1974 Arab League summit|Rabat summit conference]] in 1974, in the aftermath of the Yom-Kippur War,  and the rest of the Arab League agreed that the PLO was the "sole legitimate representative of the  people". Subsequently,  [[ disengagement from the West Bank|renounced]] its claims to the West Bank in 1988.

At the [[Madrid Conference of 1991|1991 Madrid Conference]],  agreed to negotiate a peace treaty sponsored by the US and the Soviet Union. The [[– peace treaty]] was signed on 26 October 1994. In 1997, in retribution for [[1997 Mahane Yehuda Market bombings|a bombing]],  agents entered  using  passports and poisoned [[Khaled Mashal]], a senior [[Hamas]] leader living in . Bowing to intense international pressure,  provided an antidote to the poison and released dozens of political prisoners, including [[Sheikh Ahmed Yassin]], after King Hussein threatened to annul the peace treaty.

[[File:HabisAlMajali&WasfiAlTall.jpg|thumb|right|Army Chief [[Habis Majali]] and Prime Minister [[Wasfi Tal]] during a military parade in 1970, two widely acclaimed national figures]]

On 7 February 1999, [[Abdullah II of |Abdullah II]] ascended the throne upon the death of his father Hussein, who had ruled for nearly 50 years. Abdullah embarked on economic liberalisation when he assumed the throne, and his reforms led to an economic boom which continued until 2008. Abdullah II has been credited with increasing foreign investment, improving public-private partnerships and providing the foundation for [[Aqaba]]'s free-trade zone and 's flourishing information and communication technology sector. He also set up five other special economic zones. However, during the following years 's economy experienced hardship as it dealt with the effects of the [[Great Recession]] and spillover from the [[Arab Spring]].

[[Al-Qaeda]] under [[Abu Musab al-Zarqawi]]'s leadership launched [[2005 Amman bombings|coordinated explosions]] in three hotel lobbies in Amman on 9 November 2005, resulting in 60 deaths and 115 injured. The bombings, which targeted civilians, caused widespread outrage among . The attack is considered to be a rare event in the country, and 's [[General Intelligence Directorate ()|internal security]] was dramatically improved afterwards. No major terrorist attacks have occurred since then. Abdullah and  are viewed with contempt by Islamic extremists for the country's peace treaty with , its relationship with the West, and its mostly non-religious laws.

The Arab Spring were large-scale protests that erupted in the [[Arab world]] in 2011, demanding economic and political reforms. Many of these protests tore down regimes in some Arab nations, leading to instability that ended with violent civil wars. In response to [[2011–2012  protests|domestic unrest]], Abdullah replaced his prime minister and introduced reforms including reforming the constitution and laws governing public freedoms and elections. Proportional representation was re-introduced to the  parliament in the [[2016  general election|2016 general election]], a move which he said would eventually lead to establishing parliamentary governments.  was left largely unscathed from the violence that swept the region despite an influx of 1.4&nbsp;million  refugees into the natural resources-lacking country and the emergence of the [[Islamic State of  and the Levant]] (ISIL).

On 4 April 2021, [[2021 arrests in |19 people were arrested]], including [[Prince Hamzah bin Hussein|Prince Hamzeh]], the former crown prince of , who was placed under house arrest, after having been accused of working to "destabilize" the kingdom.
== History ==
{{Main|History of }}

{{More citations needed section|date=December 2018}}<!-- and Soviet sections have few citations-->
[[File:Assimilation of Baltic and Aryan Peoples by Uralic Speakers in the Middle and Upper Volga Basin (Shaded Relief BG).png|thumb|Central Asia, including modern , during the [[Iron Age]]]]
[[File:XiongnuMap.png|thumb|The largest extent of [[Xiongnu]]'s influence in the 2nd century BC.]]

 has been inhabited since the [[Paleolithic]] era. The [[Botai culture]] (3700–3100 BC) is credited with the first domestication of horses. The Botai population derived most of their ancestry from a deeply European-related population known as [[Ancient North Eurasian]]s, while also displaying some [[Genetic history of East Asians|Ancient East Asian]] admixture. [[Pastoralism]] developed during the [[Neolithic]]. The population was [[Caucasian race|Caucasoid]] during the [[Bronze Age|Bronze]] and [[Iron Age]] period.

The  territory was a key constituent of the Eurasian trading [[Steppe Route]], the ancestor of the terrestrial [[Silk Road UNESCO World Heritage Sites|Silk Roads]]. Archaeologists believe that humans first [[domestication of the horse|domesticated the horse]] in the region's vast steppes. During recent prehistoric times, Central Asia was inhabited by groups such as the possibly Indo-European [[Afanasievo culture]], later early [[Indo-|Indo-]] cultures such as [[Andronovo culture|Andronovo]], and later Indo- such as the [[Saka]] and [[Massagetae]]. Other groups included the nomadic [[Scythians]] and the Persian [[Achaemenid Empire]] in the southern territory of the modern country. The Andronovo and [[Srubnaya culture]]s, precursors to the peoples of the [[Scythian cultures]], were found to harbor mixed ancestry from the [[Yamnaya culture|Yamnaya]] [[Western Steppe Herders|Steppe herders]] and peoples of the Central European Middle Neolithic.

In 329 BC, [[Alexander the Great]] and his [[Ancient |]] army fought in the [[Battle of Jaxartes]] against the [[Scythians]] along the Jaxartes River, now known as the [[Syr Darya]] along the southern border of modern .

=== Turkic Khaganate ===
{{main|Turkic migration|First Turkic Khaganate}}

The main [[Turkic migration|migration]] of [[Turkic peoples]] occurred between the 5th and 11th centuries when they spread across most of Central Asia. The Turkic peoples slowly replaced and assimilated the previous [[ languages|]]-speaking locals, turning the population of Central Asia from largely [[Indo-|]], into primarily of East Asian descent.

The [[First Turkic Khaganate]] was founded by [[Bumin Qaghan|Bumin]] in 552 on the  Plateau and quickly spread west toward the Caspian Sea. The [[Göktürks]] drove before them various peoples: [[Xionites]], [[Uar (tribe)|Uar]], [[Onogurs|Oghur]]s, and others. These seem to have merged into the [[Pannonian Avars|Avars]] and [[Old Great |Bulgars]]. Within 35 years, the [[Eastern Turkic Khaganate|eastern half]] and the [[Western Turkic Khaganate]] were independent. The Western Khaganate reached its peak in the early 7th century.

=== Cuman-Kipchak and the Golden Horde ===
{{main|Cumania|Golden Horde|Turco-Mongol tradition}}

[[File:Cumania (1200) eng.png|thumb|right|[[Cumania|Cuman–Kipchak confederation]] in Eurasia circa 1200. The  are descendants of [[Kipchaks]] and other Turkic and medieval Mongol tribes.]]

The [[Cuman people|Cumans]] entered the steppes of modern-day  around the early 11th century, where they later joined with the [[Kipchak people|Kipchak]] and established the vast Cuman-Kipchak confederation. While ancient cities [[Taraz]] (Aulie-Ata) and [[Hazrat-e Turkestan]] had long served as important way-stations along the [[Silk Road]] connecting Asia and Europe, true political consolidation began only with the Mongol rule of the early 13th century. Under the [[Mongol Empire]], the first strictly structured administrative districts (Ulus) were established. After the [[division of the Mongol Empire]] in 1259, the land that would become modern-day  was ruled by the [[Golden Horde]], also known as the Ulus of Jochi. During the Golden Horde period, a [[Turco-Mongol tradition]] emerged among the ruling elite wherein [[Turkification|Turkicised]] descendants of [[Genghis Khan]] followed [[Islam]] and continued to reign over the lands.

===  Khanate ===
{{Main| Khanate}}

In 15th century, the [[ Khanate]] emerged as a result of the dissolution of the [[Golden Horde]]. Established by [[Janibek Khan]] and [[Kerei Khan]], it continued to be ruled by the [[Chinggisids|Chingissid]] clan of [[Töre (dynasty)|Tore]] ([[Jochi]]d dynasty). The  Khanate was a successor state of the left (eastern) wing of Ulus Of Jochi.

[[File:Жуз.svg|thumb|250px|Approximate areas occupied by the three  jüz in the early 20th century
{|
|{{legend|#ccff99|[[Junior Juz]]}}
|{{legend|#fdd99b|[[Middle Juz]]}}
|{{legend|#ffaaaa|[[Senior Juz]]}}
|}]]

In the early 16th century,  transformed [[ Khanate|the Khanate]] into a [[nomadic empire]] stretching across the steppes east of the Caspian Sea and north of the [[Aral Sea]] as far as the upper [[Irtysh|Irtysh River]] and the western approaches to the [[Altai Mountains]]. During the reigns of [[Burunduk Khan]] (ruled 1488–1509) and [[Kasym Khan]] (1509–18), the  were the masters of virtually the entire steppe region, reportedly able to bring 200,000 cavalry into the field and feared by all their neighbours. Many historians consider Kasym Khan's leadership the starting point of a distinct and sovereign  state. His influence extended  authority from the southeastern regions of modern  to the [[Ural Mountains]]. [[Mirza Muhammad Haidar Dughlat|Mirza Muhammad Haidar]] wrote in his ''Tarikh-i-Rashidi'' that:

{{Blockquote|Kásim Khán became the absolute ruler over all of [[Cumania|Dasht-i-Kipchák]]. His army numbered more than a million men, gaining such fame and power as no one had since [[jochi|Juji Khán]].|author=[[Mirza Muhammad Haidar Dughlat]]}}

[[File:Huang Qing Zhigong Tu - 067.jpg|thumb|Noble . Portrait created by an artist of the [[Qing Dynasty]] in the second half of the 18th century]]

During the reign of the three sons of [[Kasym Khan]], the authority of the khan weakened somewhat, leading to the eventual fragmentation of the  Khanate into three distinct "hordes": the Great Horde in southeastern  north of the Tien Shan, the Middle Horde in the central steppe near the Aral Sea, and the Little Horde between the Aral Sea and the Ural River. In these regions, the khan’s power was often constrained by tribal leaders, known as sultans, and even more so by the beys and batyrs, heads of the clan-based communities. Although the khans nominally commanded a formidable military, their authority relied heavily on the loyalty of these local leaders.

The final son of Kasym Khan to rule, [[Haqnazar Khan|Haqnazar]], overcame these challenges, reunited the three hordes, and expanded his power beyond the steppes. He brought under his control not only the  hordes but also the [[Bashkirs]], [[ people|]] and [[Nogais]], as well as territories such as the [[Khanate of Kazan|Kazan]], [[Khanate of Sibir|Siberian]], and [[Astrakhan Khanate|Astrakhan khanates]], and cities like [[Bukhara]], [[Khiva]], and [[Tashkent]]. According to Rychkov, Haqnazar’s reign was marked by a brutal consolidation of power, where he exploited the instability of neighboring peoples, imposed heavy tributes, and severely restricted their movements and resources. He limited them to a single cooking pot per three households, confiscated livestock, goods, and even children, and prohibited land ownership and movement across certain rivers. These actions rendered these peoples impoverished and submissive, but they also strengthened his dominion across the region.

{{blockquote|Akhnazar, having established himself as khan, greatly expanded his power, not only ruling over the Nogais and the Bashkirs, but also conquering the Kazan, Siberian, and Astrakhan kingdoms, as well as Bukhara, Khiva, Tashkent, and many other cities, bringing them under his control and extracting tribute from them. Before his rule, the Bashkirs were governed by various regional khans: Kucum Khan of Siberia to the east, the Kazan khans controlling the lands by the Belaya and Yaik rivers, and the Nogai khans in the southern mountains. Akhnazar, however, brought them all under his dominion, consolidating power and reducing them to subjugation. He took note of their instability, disorder, and various acts of theft, relentlessly exhausting their resources and rendering them powerless. He limited them to only a single cooking pot for every three households, seized their livestock, goods, and even their children, and prohibited them from owning land or crossing the Belaya River. Those who hunted for fur were forced to pay him tribute in the form of one fox, one beaver, and one marten for each person. This constant exploitation eventually led the people to the brink of utter poverty and destitution.}}

He initiated regular raids into [[Transoxiana]], a pattern continued by his successors, including Tauekel Khan, who briefly occupied [[Samarkand]]. However, by the early 17th century, the unity that had been restored began to unravel once again, leading to the fragmentation of the khanate. As a result, central power weakened significantly, and numerous smaller, local rulers emerged across the region.

After Tauke’s death in 1715/1718, the  Khanate lost its unity, and the three hordes effectively became separate khanates.

During the 17th century, the  fought the [[Oirats]], a federation of western [[Mongols|Mongol]] tribes, including the [[Dzungar people|Dzungar]]. The beginning of the 18th century marked the zenith of the  Khanate. During this period the Little Horde participated in the 1723–1730 [[-Dzungar Wars|war against the Dzungar Khanate]], following their "Great Disaster" invasion of  territory. Under the leadership of [[Abul Khair Khan]], the  won major victories over the Dzungar at the Bulanty River in 1726 and at the [[Battle of Añyraqai]] in 1729.

[[File:Abulkhair_Khan.jpg|thumb|left|170px|[[Abul Khair Khan|Abulkhair Khan]], Khan of the Little Horde]]

[[Ablai Khan]] participated in the most significant battles against the Dzungars from the 1720s to the 1750s, for which he was declared a "''batyr''" ("hero") by the people. He later became the last khan whose authority was recognised across the  steppe, ruling the Middle Zhuz from 1771 to 1781. In 1740, Ablai had accepted  suzerainty, while pursuing a policy of balancing between [[ Empire]] and [[Qing dynasty]], which for a time allowed the  Khanate to preserve relative autonomy. After his death, his son Vali Khan (1781–1819) abandoned an independent policy and acknowledged [[ Empire|]] authority.

[[File:-1993-Bill-100-Obverse.jpg|thumb|right|200px| Abylai Khan as depicted by the artist, portrait on the obverse of the 100 tenge banknote]]
[[File:Абылай_ханның_қытай_императорына_хаты_(1760).png|thumb|Letter from Abilai to Emperor [[Qianlong Emperor|Qianlong]] in the [[Oirat language]]|150px]]

In 1822, the Khanate institution among the  [[zhuz|Hordes]] was abolished, sparking the [[Kenesary's Rebellion|uprising of Sultan Qasym]] in 1824–1827. The final attempt to restore the Khanate was led by Sultan [[Kenesary Qasymuly|Kenesary Qasymov]], who proclaimed himself khan in 1837 and conducted an armed struggle against  authorities until his death in 1847.

===   ===
[[File:Battle Cossacks with  1826.JPG|thumb|[[Ural Cossacks]] skirmish with ]]

In the first half of the 18th century, the [[ Empire]] constructed the {{Interlanguage link|Irtysh line|ru|Иртышская линия}}, a series of forty-six forts and ninety-six redoubts, including [[Omsk]] (1716), [[Semey|Semipalatinsk]] (1718), [[Pavlodar]] (1720), [[Orenburg]] (1743) and [[Petropavl]]ovsk (1752), to prevent  and Oirat raids into  territory. In the late 18th century the  took advantage of [[Pugachev's Rebellion]], which was centred on the Volga area, to raid  and [[Volga |Volga ]] settlements. In the 19th century, the [[ Empire]] began to expand its influence into Central Asia. The "[[Great Game]]" period is generally regarded as running from approximately 1813 to the [[Anglo- Convention of 1907]]. The [[tsar]]s effectively ruled over most of the territory belonging to what is now the Republic of .

The  Empire introduced an administrative system and built military garrisons and barracks in its effort to establish a presence in Central Asia in the so-called "Great Game" for dominance in the area against the [[ Empire]], which was extending its influence from the south in  and Southeast Asia.  built its first outpost, [[Orsk]], in 1735.  introduced the  language in all schools and governmental organisations.

's efforts to impose its system aroused the resentment of the , and, by the 1860s, some  resisted its rule.  had disrupted the traditional nomadic lifestyle and livestock-based economy, and people were suffering from starvation, with some  tribes being decimated. The  national movement, which began in the late 19th century, sought to preserve the native language and identity by resisting the attempts of the  Empire to assimilate and stifle  culture.

Due to its incorporation into the  Empire,  intellectuals led a cultural renaissance that surpassed those of other Central Asian peoples.  schooling brought modern ideas to the steppe, and figures like [[Shoqan Walikhanov]] and [[Abai Qunanbaiuly|Abay Kunanbayev]] adapted these ideas to the specific needs of  society, creating a secular culture unparalleled in other parts of Asian .

[[File:Valikhanov.jpg|thumb|Shoqan Walikhanov and [[Fyodor Dostoyevsky]]|200px]]
[[File:Statue of Abai Kunanbayev (Budapest) 05.jpg|thumb|right|Statue of Abai Qunanbaiuly in [[Budapest]], |200px]]

From the 1890s onward, ever-larger numbers of settlers from the  Empire began [[Colonization|colonizing]] the territory of present-day , in particular, the province of [[Jetisu|Semirechye]]. The number of settlers rose still further once the [[Trans-Aral Railway]] from [[Orenburg]] to [[Tashkent]] was completed in 1906. A specially created Migration Department (Переселенческое Управление) in [[St. Petersburg]] oversaw and encouraged the migration to expand  influence in the area. During the 19th century, about 400,000  immigrated to , and about one million Slavs, , Jews, and others immigrated to the region during the first third of the 20th century. [[Vasile Balabanov]] was the administrator responsible for the resettlement during much of this time.

The competition for land and water that ensued between the  and the newcomers caused great resentment against colonial rule during the final years of the [[ Empire]]. The most serious uprising, the [[Central Asian revolt of 1916|Central Asian revolt]], occurred in 1916. The  attacked  and [[Cossacks|Cossack]] settlers and military garrisons. The revolt resulted in a series of clashes and in brutal massacres committed by both sides. Both sides resisted the communist government until late 1919.

In the wake of the [[ Revolution]], the [[Alash Autonomy|Alash Orda]] government was formed in 1917 as an attempt to secure  autonomy. Although it existed only in name, Alash Orda represented the  push for self-rule. The Bolshevik Red Army eventually defeated White  forces in the region by 1920, and  was incorporated into the Soviet Union.

===  SSR ===
{{Main| Soviet Socialist Republic}}

Following the [[October Revolution|collapse of central government]] in [[ Petersburg|Petrograd]] in November 1917, the  (then in  officially referred to as "Kirghiz") experienced a brief period of autonomy (the [[Alash Autonomy]]) before eventually succumbing to the [[Bolsheviks]]' rule. On 26 August 1920, the [[Kirghiz Autonomous Socialist Soviet Republic (1920–25)|Kirghiz Autonomous Socialist Soviet Republic]] within the [[ Soviet Federative Socialist Republic]] (RSFSR) was established. The Kirghiz ASSR included the territory of present-day , but its administrative centre was the mainly -populated town of [[Orenburg]]. In June 1925, the Kirghiz ASSR was renamed the [[ Autonomous Soviet Socialist Republic|Kazak ASSR]] and its administrative centre was transferred to the town of [[Kyzylorda]], and in April 1927 to [[Almaty|Alma-Ata]].

Despite their traditionally nomadic lifestyle, the  were among the most literate groups in Central Asia, which set them apart in the region. However, Soviet policies, including forced [[collectivization]] and repression of the traditional elite in the late 1920s and 1930s, led to devastating consequences for the  population. These policies caused widespread famine and high fatalities, with an estimated 1.5 million deaths between 1926 and 1939, mostly due to starvation and related diseases, as well as violence (see also: [[Famine in  of 1932–33]]). Thousands of  fled to , but many did not survive the journey. During the 1930s, some members of the  intelligentsia were executed – as part of the [[Political repression in the Soviet Union|policies of political reprisals]] pursued by the Soviet government in Moscow.{{citation needed|date=May 2024}}

On 5 December 1936, the [[ Autonomous Soviet Socialist Republic]] (whose territory by then corresponded to that of modern ) was detached from the [[ Soviet Federative Socialist Republic]] (RSFSR) and made the [[ Soviet Socialist Republic]], a full [[Republics of the Soviet Union|union republic]] of the USSR, one of eleven such republics at the time, along with the [[Kirghiz Soviet Socialist Republic]].

The republic was one of the destinations for exiled and convicted persons, as well as for mass resettlements, or deportations affected by the central USSR authorities during the 1930s and 1940s, such as approximately 400,000 [[Volga ]] deported from the [[Volga  Autonomous Soviet Socialist Republic]] in September–October 1941, and then later the [[ in |]] and [[Deportation of the Crimean Tatars|Crimean Tatars]]. Deportees and prisoners were interned in some of the biggest [[Gulag|Soviet labour camps]] (the Gulag), including [[Akmol|ALZhIR]] camp outside Astana, which was reserved for the wives of men considered "enemies of the people". Many moved due to the policy of [[population transfer in the Soviet Union]] and others were forced into [[involuntary settlements in the Soviet Union]].

The [[Eastern Front (World War II)|Soviet- War]] (1941–1945) led to an increase in industrialisation and [[mineral extraction]] in support of the war effort. At the time of [[Joseph Stalin]]'s death in 1953, however,  still had an overwhelmingly agricultural economy. In 1953, Soviet leader [[Nikita Khrushchev]] initiated the [[Virgin Lands Campaign]] designed to turn the traditional pasturelands of  into a major grain-producing region for the Soviet Union. The Virgin Lands policy brought mixed results. However, along with later modernisations under Soviet leader [[Leonid Brezhnev]] (in power 1964–1982), it accelerated the development of the agricultural sector, which remains the source of livelihood for a large percentage of 's population. Because of the decades of privation, war and resettlement, by 1959 the [[]] had become a minority, making up 30% of the population. Ethnic [[]] accounted for 43%.

In 1947, the USSR, as part of its [[Soviet atomic bomb project|atomic bomb project]], founded an [[Semipalatinsk Test Site|atomic bomb test site]] near the north-eastern town of [[Semey|Semipalatinsk]], where the [[RDS-1|first Soviet nuclear bomb]] test was conducted in 1949. Hundreds of nuclear tests were conducted until 1989 with adverse consequences for the nation's environment and population. The [[Anti-nuclear movement in ]] became a major political force in the late 1980s.

In April 1961, [[Baikonur]] became the springboard of [[Vostok 1]], a spacecraft with Soviet cosmonaut [[Yuri Gagarin]] being the first human to enter space.

In December 1986, mass demonstrations by young ethnic , later called the [[Jeltoqsan]] riot, took place in Almaty to protest the replacement of the [[General Secretary|First Secretary]] of the [[Communist Party of |Communist Party]] of the  SSR [[Dinmukhamed Konayev]] with [[Gennady Kolbin]] from the [[ SFSR]]. Governmental troops suppressed the unrest, several people were killed, and many demonstrators were jailed. In the waning days of Soviet rule, discontent continued to grow and found expression under Soviet leader [[Mikhail Gorbachev]]'s policy of ''[[glasnost]]'' ("openness").
