All other state governments remained loyal to [[Union ( Civil War)|the Union]].{{efn|The Confederate States of America was formed by the following states, each state government of which formally declared its [[Secession in the |secession]] from the : [[South Carolina in the  Civil War|South Carolina]], [[Mississippi in the  Civil War|Mississippi]], [[Florida in the  Civil War|Florida]], [[Alabama in the  Civil War|Alabama]], [[ in the  Civil War|]], [[Louisiana in the  Civil War|Louisiana]], [[Texas in the  Civil War|Texas]], [[Virginia in the  Civil War|Virginia]], [[Arkansas in the  Civil War|Arkansas]], [[Tennessee in the  Civil War|Tennessee]], and [[North Carolina in the  Civil War|North Carolina]].}}{{Sfn|McPherson|1988|p=236}} War broke out in April 1861 after the Confederacy [[Battle of Fort Sumter|bombarded Fort Sumter]].{{Sfn|McPherson|1988|pp=273–274}} Following the [[Emancipation Proclamation]] on January 1, 1863, many freed slaves joined the [[Union army]]. The war [[Turning point of the  Civil War|began to turn in the Union's favor]] following the 1863 [[Siege of Vicksburg]] and [[Battle of Gettysburg]], and the Confederates surrendered in 1865 after the Union's victory in the [[Battle of Appomattox Court House]].

=== Reconstruction, Gilded Age, and Progressive Era (1863&ndash;1917) ===
{{Main|History of the  (1865–1917)}}
[[File:Emigrants (i.e. immigrants) landing at Ellis Island -.webm|thumb|An [[Edison Studios]] film showing immigrants arriving at [[Ellis Island]] in [[New York Harbor]], a major point of entry for European [[Immigration to the |immigrants]] in the late 19th and early 20th centuries]]

Efforts toward [[Reconstruction era|reconstruction in the secessionist South]] had begun as early as 1862,{{sfn|Dubois|1935|p=163}} but it was only after [[Assassination of Abraham Lincoln|President Lincoln's assassination]] that the three [[Reconstruction Amendments]] to the Constitution were ratified [[Civil rights movement (1865–1896)|to protect civil rights]]. The amendments codified nationally the abolition of slavery and involuntary servitude except as punishment for crimes, promised equal protection under the law for all persons, and prohibited discrimination on the basis of race or previous enslavement.{{sfn|Foner|1988|p=66-7, 251-261, 446-449}} As a result, African  took an active political role in ex-Confederate states in the decade following the Civil War.{{sfn|Foner|1988|p=xxv}}{{sfn|Dubois|1935|p=381-727}} The former Confederate states were readmitted to the Union, beginning with Tennessee in 1866 and ending with  in 1870.

National infrastructure, including [[First transcontinental telegraph|transcontinental telegraph]] and [[First transcontinental railroad|railroads]], spurred growth in the [[ frontier]]. This was accelerated by the [[Homestead Acts]], through which nearly 10 percent of the total land area of the  was given away free to some 1.6 million homesteaders. From 1865 through 1917, an unprecedented stream of [[immigrants]] arrived in the , including 24.4 million from Europe. Most came through the [[Port of New York and New Jersey|Port of New York]], and New York City and other large cities on the [[East Coast of the |East Coast]] became home to large [[History of the Jews in the |Jewish]], [[ |]], and [[ |]] populations. Many [[Northern Europe|Northern Europeans]] as well as significant numbers of [[ |]] and other [[Central Europe|Central Europeans]] moved to the [[Midwestern |Midwest]]. At the same time, about one million [[- | ]] migrated from [[Quebec]] to [[New England]]. During the [[Great Migration (African )|Great Migration]], millions of African  [[Jim Crow economy|left the rural South]] for urban areas in the North. [[Alaska Purchase|Alaska was purchased]] from [[ Empire|]] in 1867.

The [[Compromise of 1877]] is generally considered the end of the Reconstruction era, as it resolved the electoral crisis following the [[1876  presidential election|1876 presidential election]] and led President [[Rutherford B. Hayes]] to reduce the role of federal troops in the South.{{sfn|Foner|1988|p=xxvii, 575-587}} Immediately, the [[Redeemers]] began evicting the [[Carpetbaggers]] and quickly regained local control of Southern politics in the name of [[white supremacy]]. African  endured a period of heightened, overt racism following Reconstruction, a time often called the [[nadir of  race relations]]. A series of Supreme Court decisions, including ''[[Plessy v. Ferguson]]'', emptied the [[Fourteenth Amendment to the  Constitution|Fourteenth]] and [[Fifteenth Amendment to the  Constitution|Fifteenth Amendment]]s of their force, allowing [[Jim Crow laws]] in the South to remain unchecked, [[sundown town]]s in the Midwest, and [[Racial segregation in the |segregation in communities across the country]], which would be reinforced by the policy of [[redlining]] later adopted by the federal [[Home Owners' Loan Corporation]].

[[Second Industrial Revolution|An explosion of technological advancement]] accompanied by the exploitation of cheap immigrant labor led to [[Gilded Age|rapid economic expansion during the late 19th and early 20th centuries]], allowing the  to outpace the economies of England, , and  combined. This fostered the amassing of power by [[Robber baron (industrialist)|a few prominent industrialists]], largely by their formation of [[Trust (business)|trusts]] and [[Monopoly|monopolies]] to prevent competition. [[Tycoon]]s led the nation's expansion in the [[History of rail transportation in the |railroad]], [[History of the petroleum industry in the |petroleum]], and [[History of the steel industry (1850–1970)|steel]] industries. The  emerged as a pioneer of the [[Automotive industry in the |automotive industry]]. These changes resulted in significant increases in [[economic inequality]], [[How the Other Half Lives|slum conditions]], and [[List of incidents of civil unrest in the |social unrest]], creating the environment for [[Labor history of the |labor unions]] and [[History of the socialist movement in the |socialist movements]] to begin to flourish. This period eventually ended with the advent of the [[Progressive Era]], which was characterized by significant reforms.

Pro- elements in Hawaii [[Overthrow of the Hawaiian Kingdom|overthrew the Hawaiian monarchy]]; the islands [[Newlands Resolution|were annexed]] in 1898. That same year, [[Puerto Rico]], [[the ]], and [[Guam]] were ceded to the U.S. by  after the latter's defeat in the [[– War]]. (The  was granted full independence from the U.S. on July 4, 1946, following World War II. Puerto Rico and Guam have remained U.S. territories.) [[ ]] was acquired by the  in 1900 after the [[Second  Civil War]]. The [[ Virgin Islands|U.S. Virgin Islands]] were purchased from [[]] in 1917.

=== World War I, Great Depression, and World War II (1917&ndash;1945) ===
{{Main|History of the  (1917–1945)}}
[[File:Trinity shot color (4x3 cropped).jpg|thumb|The 1945  [[Trinity (nuclear test)|Trinity test]], the first-ever detonation of a [[nuclear weapon]]]]

The  [[ entry into World War I|entered World War I]] alongside the [[Allies of World War I|Allies]] in 1917 helping to turn the tide against the [[Central Powers]]. In 1920, [[Nineteenth Amendment to the  Constitution|a constitutional amendment]] granted nationwide [[Women's suffrage in the |women's suffrage]]. During the 1920s and 1930s, radio for [[mass communication]] and early television transformed communications nationwide.{{sfn|Winchester|2013|pp=410–411}} The [[Wall Street Crash of 1929]] triggered the [[Great Depression in the |Great Depression]], to which President [[Franklin D. Roosevelt]] responded with the [[New Deal]] plan of "reform, recovery and relief", a series of unprecedented and sweeping [[Alphabet agencies|recovery programs]] and [[Works Progress Administration|employment relief projects]] combined with [[Regulatory economics|financial reforms and regulations]].

[[ non-interventionism before entering World War II|Initially neutral]] during [[Military history of the  during World War II|World War II]], the U.S. began [[Lend-Lease|supplying war materiel]] to the [[Allies of World War II]] in March 1941 and [[ entry into World War II|entered the war]] in December after [[Empire of |]]'s [[attack on Pearl Harbor]]. The U.S. [[Manhattan Project|developed the first nuclear weapons]] and [[Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki|used them against the  cities of Hiroshima and Nagasaki]] in August 1945, ending the war. The  was one of the "[[Four Policemen]]" who met to plan the [[Aftermath of World War II|post-war world]], alongside the [[]], the [[Soviet Union]], and [[Republic of  (1912–1949)|]].{{sfn|Hoopes|Brinkley|1997|p=100}}{{sfn|Gaddis|1972|p=25}} The U.S. emerged relatively unscathed from the war, with even greater [[economic power]] and [[Sphere of influence|international political influence]].

=== Cold War and social revolution (1945&ndash;1991) ===
{{Main|History of the  (1945&ndash;1964)|History of the  (1964–1980)|History of the  (1980–1991)}}
[[File:Civil Rights March on Washington, D.C. (Leaders marching from the Washington Monument to the Lincoln Memorial) - NARA - 542010.jpg|thumb|Civil rights activists during the [[March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom]] in [[Washington, D.C.]] in August 1963]]

The end of [[World War II]] in 1945 left the U.S. and the [[Soviet Union]] as [[superpower]]s, each with its own political, military, and economic [[sphere of influence]]. Geopolitical tensions between the two superpowers soon led to the [[Cold War]]. The U.S. utilized the policy of [[containment]] to limit the Soviet Union's sphere of influence, engaged in [[ involvement in regime change#1945–1991: Cold War|regime change]] against governments perceived to be aligned with the Soviets, and prevailed in the [[Space Race]], which culminated with the [[Apollo 11|first crewed Moon landing]] in 1969.

Domestically, the U.S. [[Post–World War II economic expansion|experienced economic growth]], [[Urbanization in the |urbanization]], and [[Mid-20th century baby boom|population growth following World War II]].{{sfn|Winchester|2013|pp=305–308}} The [[civil rights movement]] emerged, with [[Martin Luther King Jr.]] becoming a prominent leader in the early 1960s. The [[Great Society]] plan of President [[Lyndon B. Johnson]]'s administration resulted in groundbreaking and broad-reaching laws, policies and [[Twenty-fourth Amendment to the  Constitution|a constitutional amendment]] to counteract some of the worst effects of lingering [[institutional racism]].

The [[Counterculture of the 1960s|counterculture movement]] in the U.S. brought significant social changes, including the liberalization of attitudes toward [[recreational drug use]] and [[Sexual revolution|sexuality]]. It also encouraged [[Draft evasion in the  War|open defiance of the military draft]] (leading to the [[Conscription in the |end of conscription]] in 1973) and [[Opposition to  involvement in the  War|wide opposition]] to [[ in the  War|U.S. intervention in ]], with the U.S. totally withdrawing in 1975. [[Women's liberation movement in the |A societal shift in the roles of women]] was significantly responsible for the large increase in female paid labor participation during the 1970s, and by 1985 the majority of  women aged 16 and older were employed.

The [[Revolutions of 1989|Fall of Communism]] and the [[dissolution of the Soviet Union]] from 1989 to 1991 marked the [[end of the Cold War]] and [[Superpower#After the Cold War|left the  as the world's sole superpower]]. This cemented the ' global influence, reinforcing the concept of the "[[ Century]]" as the U.S. dominated international political, cultural, economic, and military affairs.

=== Contemporary (1991&ndash;present) ===
{{Main|History of the  (1991–2016)|History of the  (2016–present)}}
[[File:WTC smoking on 9-11.jpeg|thumb|The [[World Trade Center (1973–2001)|Twin Towers]] in New York City during the [[September 11 attacks]] of 2001]]

The 1990s saw the [[1990s  boom|longest recorded economic expansion in  history]], a dramatic [[Crime in the #Crime over time|decline in U.S. crime rates]], and [[Technological and industrial history of the #Computers and information networks|advances in technology]]. Throughout this decade, technological innovations such as the [[World Wide Web]], the evolution of the [[Pentium (original)|Pentium]] microprocessor in accordance with [[Moore's law]], rechargeable [[Lithium-ion battery|lithium-ion batteries]], the first [[gene therapy]] trial, and [[cloning]] either emerged in the U.S. or were improved upon there. The [[Human Genome Project]] was formally launched in 1990, while [[Nasdaq]] became the first stock market in the  to trade online in 1998.
 
In the [[Gulf War]] of 1991, [[Coalition of the Gulf War|an -led international coalition of states]] expelled an [[Ba'athist |]] invasion force that had occupied neighboring [[]]. The [[September 11 attacks]] on the  in 2001 by the [[Pan-Islamism|pan-Islamist]] militant organization [[al-Qaeda]] led to the [[war on terror]] and subsequent [[War in  (2001–2021)|military interventions in ]] and [[ War|in ]]. 
 
The [[2000s  housing bubble|U.S. housing bubble]] culminated in 2007 with the [[Great Recession]], the largest economic contraction since the Great Depression. In the 2010s and early 2020s, the  has experienced increased [[Political polarization in the |political polarization]] and [[Democratic backsliding in the |democratic backsliding]]. The country's polarization was violently reflected in the [[January 6  Capitol attack|January 2021 Capitol attack]], when a mob of insurrectionists entered the [[ Capitol|U.S. Capitol]] and sought to prevent the peaceful transfer of power in an [[Self-coup#Notable events described as attempted self-coups|attempted self-coup d'état]].
== History ==
{{Main|History of }}

=== Prehistoric  ===
{{Main|Prehistoric }}
[[File:Predynastic collage.png|thumb|'''Clockwise''': a [[Badarian culture|Badarian]] mortuary figurine,  a [[Naqada culture|Naqada]] jar, a [[Naqada culture|Naqada]] statuette of the goddess [[Bat (goddess)|Bat]], the [[Cosmetic palette|Four dogs palette]],  the [[Gebel el-Arak Knife]], and a [[Naqada culture|Naqada]] diorite vase.]]
Evidence of [[rock carvings]] along the [[Nile]] and in surrounding oases indicates early habitation. In the [[10th millennium BCE]], a culture of [[hunter-gatherer]]s and [[fishing|fishers]] was replaced by a [[cereal|grain]]-grinding culture. Climate changes or [[overgrazing]] around 8000 [[before common era|BCE]] began to desiccate the pastoral lands of , forming the [[Sahara]]. Early [[tribal people]]s migrated to the Nile River where they developed a settled agricultural [[Economic system|economy]] and more centralised society.

By about 6000 BCE, a [[Neolithic]] culture took root in the Nile Valley. During the Neolithic era, several predynastic cultures developed independently in [[Upper and Lower ]]. The [[Badarian]] culture and the successor [[Naqada]] series are generally regarded as precursors to [[Ancient |dynastic ]]. The earliest known Lower  site, Merimda, predates the Badarian by about seven hundred years. Contemporaneous Lower  communities coexisted with their southern counterparts for more than two thousand years, remaining culturally distinct, but maintaining frequent contact through trade. The earliest known evidence of [[ hieroglyphs| hieroglyphic]] inscriptions appeared during the predynastic period on Naqada III pottery vessels, dated to about 3200 BCE.

===Ancient  (3150 BCE–305 BCE)===
{{Main|Ancient }}
[[File:All Gizah Pyramids.jpg|thumb|right|The [[Giza Necropolis]] is the oldest of the [[Seven Wonders of the Ancient World|ancient Wonders]] and the only one still in existence.]]

Around {{circa|3150}} BCE, King [[Menes]] unified , establishing a succession of [[List of  dynasties|dynasties]] that ruled for three millennia. [[Culture of | civilisation]] thrived with distinctive achievements in [[Ancient  religion|religion]], [[Art of Ancient |art]], and [[ language|writing]]. The [[Old Kingdom]] ({{circa|2700–2200}} BCE) saw the construction of the [[ pyramids|pyramids]], including those at [[Giza Necropolis|Giza]]. A brief [[First Intermediate Period of |interregnum]] followed, succeeded by the [[Middle Kingdom of |Middle Kingdom]] ({{circa|2040}} BCE), a phase of renewed stability and prosperity under rulers such as [[Amenemhat III]].

After the [[Second Intermediate Period of |Second Intermediate Period]] and the [[Hyksos]] occupation,  was reunified by [[Ahmose I]], founder of the [[Eighteenth dynasty of |Eighteenth Dynasty]] and the [[New Kingdom of |New Kingdom]] ({{circa|1550–1070}} BCE). This era marked 's height as a major power in the region, extending influence into [[]] and the [[Levant]]. It produced many of 's most renowned [[Pharaoh]]s, [[Hatshepsut]], [[Thutmose III]], [[Akhenaten]], [[Tutankhamun]], and [[Ramesses II]], and witnessed the rise of [[Atenism]], one of the earliest forms of [[monotheism]]. Despite later invasions by [[Ancient |]], [[Kingdom of Kush|Nubians]], and [[Assyria]]ns, native dynasties eventually reasserted control.
[[File:BD Weighing of the Heart.jpg|thumb|The Weighing of the Heart from the ''[[Papyrus of Ani|Book of the Dead of Ani]]'']]
In 525 BCE, [[Cambyses II of Persia]] conquered , beginning the [[Achaemenid Empire|Achaemenid]] [[satrapy]] period (the [[Twenty-seventh Dynasty of |Twenty-seventh Dynasty]]). Although several revolts occurred,  remained under Persian control until briefly regaining independence before falling again in 343 BCE. The [[Thirtieth dynasty of |Thirtieth Dynasty]] was the last native royal house. Following renewed Persian domination, [[Alexander the Great]] conquered  in 332 BCE, after which his general [[Ptolemy I Soter]] established the [[Ptolemaic dynasty]].

===Ptolemaic and Roman  (305 BCE–641 CE)===
{{Main|Ptolemaic Kingdom|Roman }}

[[File:Denderah3 Cleopatra Cesarion.jpg|thumb|upright=0.75|The Ptolemaic Queen [[Cleopatra VII]] and her son by Julius Caesar, [[Caesarion]], at the [[Dendera Temple complex|Temple of Dendera]]]]

The [[Ptolemaic Kingdom]] was a [[Hellenistic civilization|Hellenistic]] state from southern  to [[Cyrene, |Cyrene]] and south to , with [[Alexandria]] as its capital and a centre of [[|]] culture and trade. The Ptolemies adopted pharaonic traditions to legitimize their rule, appearing on monuments in  style and participating in local religious life. The [[Lighthouse of Alexandria]], built c. 280 BCE, was one of the [[Seven Wonders of the Ancient World]], later destroyed by earthquakes. The last ruler, [[Cleopatra VII]], committed suicide after [[Octavian]] captured Alexandria, ending the dynasty and paving the way for Roman annexation. Native rebellions and dynastic disputes weakened the kingdom, facilitating its annexation by Rome.

 was a wealthy imperial province of the [[Roman Empire]], supplying grain and hosting the major city of Alexandria. Governed with Roman administration and Hellenistic culture, its population primarily spoke [[Koine |]] in major cities and [[Coptic language|Coptic ]] in rural areas. Christianity reached  in the 1st century, brought by [[ Mark the Evangelist]]. During [[Diocletian]]'s reign (284–305 CE), the [[New Testament]] had been translated into  and many  Christians were persecuted. By CE 451, a distinct [[Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria|Coptic Church]] was firmly established.

=== Middle Ages (641–1517) ===
{{Main| in the Middle Ages}}{{multiple image
| width             = 200
| image1            = C9B5617-Pano.jpg
| image2            = ساحة مسجد احمد بن طولون.jpg
| footer            = The [[Al-Hakim Mosque]] in Cairo, of [[Al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah]], the sixth [[Fatimid]] caliph
| caption2          = The [[Ibn Tulun Mosque]] in Cairo, of [[Ahmad Ibn Tulun]]
| caption1          = The [[Amr ibn al-As]] mosque in Cairo, recognised as the oldest in Africa
| direction         = vertical
| align             = right
| alt2              = The [[Ibn Tulun Mosque]] in Cairo, of [[Ahmad Ibn Tulun]]
| image3            = مسـجد الحاكم بأمر الله 06.jpg
| alt1              =
}}
The Byzantines regained control of  after a brief [[Sasanian Empire|Sasanid Persian]] invasion early in the 7th century, until 639–42, when the country was conquered by Arab Muslim forces under [[Amr ibn al-As]]. The Arabs defeated the Byzantine armies, bringing [[Islam]] to . Alexandria briefly returned to Byzantine control in 645 but fell again to the Arabs in 646. In 654, an invasion fleet sent by [[Constans II]] was repulsed. The Arabs founded [[Fustat]], later replaced by Cairo in 969.

Under the [[Abbasid]] caliphate,  was governed through deputies residing in Baghdad. Revolts occurred frequently, including the  revolt of 828 and the uprising of 831 when Copts joined Muslims against the government. Semi-independent dynasties arose, including the [[Tulunid dynasty]] (868–905) and [[Ikhshidid dynasty]] (935–969), which maintained Abbasid allegiance while exercising local authority.

The [[Fatimid Caliphate]] ruled  from the 10th century, with [[Cairo]] as their capital. After the Fatimids, the [[Ayyubid dynasty]] governed until 1250, when the [[Mamluk]]s, a military caste of [[Turkic people|Turco]]-[[Circassians|Circassian]] origin, took control. The Mamluks ruled  for the next three centuries and maintained control over parts of the Levant. By the late 13th century,  linked trade routes connecting the Red Sea with , Malaya, and the East Indies. The mid-14th century [[Black Death]] killed about 40% of 's population.

=== Ottoman  (1517–1867) ===
{{Main| Eyalet|History of  under the Muhammad Ali dynasty}}
 was conquered by the [[Ottoman Turks]] in 1517, following the defeat of the [[Mamluk Sultanate]], and became a province of the [[Ottoman Empire]]. The Mamluks, who had dominated 's military and political life for centuries, retained influence under Ottoman rule, creating a semi-autonomous power structure. The Ottomans faced repeated challenges in maintaining control, while plagues and famines weakened the economy and civil society. Between 1687 and 1731,  experienced six major famines, including the 1784 famine that killed roughly one-sixth of the population.
[[File:Louis-François Baron Lejeune 001.jpg|thumb|Napoleon defeated the [[Mamluk]] troops in the [[Battle of the Pyramids]], 21 July 1798, painted by [[Louis-François, Baron Lejeune|Lejeune]].|230x230px|left]]
In 1798, [[Napoleon Bonaparte]] invaded , defeating the Mamluks at the [[Battle of the Pyramids]]. The  occupation was short-lived, but it destabilized the region and set the stage for Muhammad Ali Pasha's rise. After the  were expelled, power struggles ensued between the Ottomans, the Mamluks, and  mercenaries in Ottoman service, leaving  politically fragmented.
{{multiple image
|length            = 200
| image1            = ModernEgypt, Muhammad Ali by Auguste Couder, BAP 17996.jpg
| image2            =  under Muhammad Ali Dynasty map en.png
| footer            = [[Muhammad Ali of |Muhammad Ali]], founder of the [[Muhammad Ali dynasty]], and the extent of their realm.
| direction         = vertical
| align             = right
}}
In 1805, [[Muhammad Ali of |Muhammad Ali Pasha]] seized power, massacring the remaining Mamluks and establishing a dynasty that would rule  until 1952. He reorganized the army along European lines, introduced conscription, and developed a centralized administration. At the same time, he promoted cash-crop agriculture, especially long-staple cotton, to integrate  into global markets. His successors, including [[Ibrahim Pasha of |Ibrahim Pasha]], [[Abbas I of |Abbas I]], [[Sa'id of |Sa'id]], and [[Isma'il Pasha of |Isma'il Pasha]], continued reforms in agriculture, science, and industry, and abolished slavery.

Muhammad Ali expanded 's control over [[Northern ]] (1820–1824), [[]] (1833), and parts of [[Arabia]] and [[]], but European powers intervened in 1841, forcing him to relinquish most of his conquests. He modernized 's infrastructure, built factories and irrigation networks, and strengthened the military, while broader education remained largely limited to military and technical training. The centralization of power and focus on military and economic modernization laid the foundation for 's transformation into a regional power.

=== Monarchical  (1867–1952) ===
{{Main|Khedivate of  {{!}} Khedivate of  (1867–1914|Sultanate of  {{!}} Sultanate of  (1914–1922)|Kingdom of  (1922–1953)}}

In 1867,  was formally granted autonomous status as a [[Khedivate of |vassal state]] of the Ottoman Empire. The [[Suez Canal]], completed in 1869 with  assistance, became a key strategic and commercial asset. Financial mismanagement and mounting debts led Isma'il Pasha to sell 's shares in the canal to Britain in 1875, increasing European influence. Rising discontent culminated in the [[Urabi revolt]], after which Britain occupied  in 1882, establishing a de facto protectorate while maintaining nominal Ottoman sovereignty. The Anglo- Condominium Agreement of 1899 placed  under joint  and  administration, though Britain retained real control. Incidents such as the [[Denshawai incident]] in 1906 intensified nationalist sentiment, laying the groundwork for political movements that challenged both Ottoman and European dominance.

[[File:Cairo-Demonstrations1919.jpg|thumb|upright=0.75|Female nationalists demonstrating in [[Cairo]], 1919|304x304px]]
In 1914, as the [[Ottoman Empire]] joined World War I alongside the Central Powers, Khedive [[Abbas II of |Abbas II]] declared support for the Ottoman side. In response, the  deposed him and installed his brother [[Hussein Kamel of |Hussein Kamel]], who assumed the title of [[Sultanate of |Sultan of ]].  was formally declared independent from the Ottoman Empire but remained under  protection.

After [[World War I]], nationalist sentiments surged. [[Saad Zaghlul]] and the [[Wafd Party]] secured popular support, but the  exiled Zaghlul and his colleagues to  on 8 March 1919, prompting the [[ revolution of 1919|first modern revolution]]. This uprising pressured the UK to issue a [[Unilateral Declaration of  Independence|declaration of independence]] on 22 February 1922. Sultan [[Fuad I of |Fuad I]] then assumed the title of King of . Despite nominal independence, Britain retained military presence and political influence.
[[File:Fuad I of , Edward VIII, & Mohamed Sa'id Paşa.jpg|thumb|[[Fuad I of ]] with [[Edward VIII|Edward, Prince of Wales]], 1932|left|223x223px]]
In 1923, a new constitution established a [[Parliamentary system|parliamentary]] government. The Wafd Party won a decisive victory in the 1923–24 elections, with [[Saad Zaghloul]] becoming prime minister. The [[Anglo- Treaty of 1936|1936 treaty]] led to  troop withdrawal from most of , except the Suez Canal. The treaty left the status of [[Anglo- |]] unresolved, as real control remained with Britain.

During [[World War II]],  served as a strategic base for [[Allies of World War II|Allied]] operations, particularly in [[North Africa]]. Although  declared martial law and severed diplomatic relations with [[Axis powers]], the  army did not engage directly. Political tensions persisted, highlighted by the 1942 [[Abdeen Palace Incident of 1942|Abdeen Palace Incident]], in which  forces pressured King Farouk to install a Wafd-coalition government.
[[File:1stAlameinBritDefense.jpg|thumb| infantry near [[First Battle of El Alamein|El Alamein]], 17 July 1942|right|200x200px]]
After the war, nationalist and anti- sentiments intensified.  troops largely withdrew in 1947, leaving a residual presence around the Suez Canal. 's defeat in the [[First Arab- War]] fueled anti-monarchy feeling. The Wafd Party's 1950 election victory forced King Farouk to appoint [[Mostafa El-Nahas]] as prime minister. In 1951,  unilaterally renounced the [[Anglo- treaty of 1936|1936 treaty]] and demanded  troop withdrawal. The situation around the Suez Canal escalated, culminating in violent confrontations that led to the killing of 43  policemen in 1952 by  troops. The Ismailia incident outraged  and led to the subsequent [[Cairo fire|Black Saturday]] anti- riots, which saw widespread destruction in Cairo.

These events precipitated the [[ Revolution of 1952|Free Officers Movement coup]] on 22–23 July 1952, led by [[Muhammad Naguib]] and [[Gamal Abdel Nasser]]. King Farouk abdicated in favor of his infant son, [[Fuad II of |Fuad II]], but real power rested with the [[ Revolutionary Command Council]]. By 18 June 1953, the monarchy was formally abolished, the 1923 constitution suspended, and  was declared a republic, with Naguib as president and Nasser as prime minister.

=== Republican  under Nasser (1952–1970) ===
{{Main|History of republican |History of  under Gamal Abdel Nasser}}
[[File:Port Said from air.jpg|thumb|Smoke rises from oil tanks beside the [[Suez Canal]] hit during the initial [[Suez Crisis|Anglo- assault]] on , 5 November 1956.]]
Following the [[ revolution of 1952|1952 Revolution]] led by the [[Free Officers Movement ()|Free Officers Movement]],  came under military control, and all political parties were banned. On 18 June 1953, the [[Republic of  (1953–1958)|Republic of ]] was officially declared, with [[Muhammad Naguib]] serving as the first President. His presidency lasted less than a year and a half, as [[Gamal Abdel Nasser]], a [[Pan-Arabist]] and the principal architect of the 1952 movement, gradually consolidated power. Naguib was forced to resign in 1954 and placed under [[house arrest]]. The presidency remained vacant until Nasser was formally elected in 1956.

In October 1954,  and the  agreed to end the [[Anglo- |Anglo- Condominium Agreement]] of 1899, granting  full independence, which came into effect on 1 January 1956. In June 1956, Nasser assumed the presidency and immediately became the central figure in 's domestic and foreign policy.  forces completed their withdrawal from the Suez Canal Zone on 13 June 1956. Later that year, on 26 July, Nasser [[nationalised]] the Suez Canal, provoking the [[Suez Crisis]] when , with support from  and the , invaded the Sinai Peninsula and targeted the Canal. The conflict ended following diplomatic pressure from the  and the Soviet Union, restoring the pre-war status quo.
[[File:Nasser in Mansoura, 1960.jpg|thumb| President [[Gamal Abdel Nasser]] in [[Mansoura, |Mansoura]], 1960]]
In 1958,  formed a political union with , creating the [[United Arab Republic]]. The union, also loosely connected with [[Mutawakkilite Kingdom of |North ]] in the [[United Arab States]], was short-lived;  seceded in 1961. During this period,  became heavily involved in the [[North  Civil War]], with military interventions and peace conferences ultimately leading to a prolonged stalemate.
In May 1967, tensions with  escalated after warnings from the Soviet Union, deployment of  forces to Sinai, expulsion of UN peacekeepers, and closure of the [[Straits of Tiran]]. These measures precipitated the [[Third Arab- War|Six-Day War]], during which  captured the [[Sinai Peninsula]] and the [[Gaza Strip]]. In response to the conflict, an [[Emergency law in |Emergency Law]] was enacted, greatly expanding police powers, restricting constitutional rights, and legalising censorship; it remained in force until 2012, except for a brief break in 1980–81.

Socially and economically, Nasser's policies transformed . At the time of the monarchy's fall, less than half a million  were considered upper class, four million were middle class, and seventeen million were lower class or poor. Education was expanded, with school enrolment more than doubling from 1953 to 1966. Land reforms, industrial support, and growth in public-sector employment created a larger middle class, including doctors, engineers, teachers, lawyers, and journalists. However, by the late 1960s, the  economy faced stagnation, political freedoms had declined, and Nasser's personal popularity began to wane.

===  under Sadat and Mubarak (1971–2011) ===
{{Main|History of  under Anwar Sadat|History of  under Hosni Mubarak}}
[[File: Armor.jpg|thumb| tanks advancing in the Sinai desert during the [[Yom Kippur War]], 1973]]
After the death of [[Gamal Abdel Nasser]] in 1970, [[Anwar Sadat]] assumed the presidency of . Sadat consolidated his power by marginalising Nasserist and leftist factions, while controlling opposition both secular and religious. He shifted 's Cold War alignment from the Soviet Union to the , expelling Soviet advisors in 1972, and renamed the country the Arab Republic of  in 1971. Sadat launched the [[Infitah]] ("open door") economic reforms. Some measures of this economic liberalization caused social tensions, most notably the removal of food subsidies in 1977 which sparked widespread [[1977  bread riots|bread riots]].
[[File:Begin, Carter and Sadat at Camp David 1978.jpg|thumb|Celebrating the signing of the 1978 [[Camp David Accords]]: [[Menachem Begin]], [[Jimmy Carter]], [[Anwar Sadat]]]]
In 1973, Sadat coordinated with  in the [[Fourth Arab- War]] to reclaim the [[Sinai Peninsula]] from  occupation. Though militarily mixed, the war restored Arab morale and strengthened Sadat's domestic legitimacy. His historic 1977 visit to Jerusalem led to the 1979 [[– peace treaty|- peace treaty]], with  withdrawing from Sinai and  recognizing  as a sovereign state. This initiative provoked widespread controversy across the Arab world, resulting in 's temporary expulsion from the [[Arab League]], but remained popular domestically. Sadat was assassinated in 1981 by an Islamic extremist opposed to his domestic policies and peace initiative.
[[File:Hosni Mubarak - Official Photo.JPG|left|thumb|222x222px|[[Hosni Mubarak]], president of  from 1981 to 2011]]
[[Hosni Mubarak]] succeeded Sadat in a 1981 referendum in which he was the sole candidate. He maintained 's peace treaty with  and improved relations with Arab neighbors. Domestically, he faced widespread poverty, high unemployment, and urban overcrowding. The 1986 Security Police riots, sparked by reports of extended military service, led to violent protests, destruction of businesses, and 107 deaths.

Terrorist attacks, particularly by Islamist groups like [[Al-Gama'a al-Islamiyya]], targeted government officials, foreigners, and Christian [[Copts]], devastating tourism, 's primary source of hard currency. The political scene was dominated by the [[National Democratic Party ()|NDP]], which curtailed freedoms of association, expression, and political participation through laws such as the 1993 Syndicates Law, 1995 Press Law, and 1999 NGOs Law.
[[File:Cairo north.JPG|thumb|Cairo grew into a [[metropolitan area]] with a population of over 22 million.]]
In 1997, the [[Luxor massacre]] left 62 dead, mostly tourists, highlighting the continuing threat to security and the economy. In 2005, limited reforms allowed multi-candidate presidential elections, but restrictions on candidates and alleged government interference ensured Mubarak's easy reelection. Voter turnout was less than 25%, and opposition leader [[Ayman Nour]] was subsequently imprisoned.

[[Human Rights Watch]] and [[Amnesty International]] reported widespread human rights abuses, including torture, arbitrary detention, and the use of  as an international "torture hub" in the context of the [[war on terror]]. Constitutional changes in 2007 further expanded presidential powers, restricted religious parties, and broadened police authority.  remained under strong military influence, described by officials as a "pharaonic" system, with democracy as a distant aspiration.

=== Contemporary (2011–present) ===
{{Main|2011  revolution|| Crisis (2011–2014)||2013  protests|2013  coup d'état}}
{{multiple image
| width             = 200
| direction         = vertical
| footer            = '''Top:''' Celebrations in [[Tahrir Square]] after the announcement of [[Hosni Mubarak]]'s resignation, 2011.<br />'''Bottom:''' Protests in Tahrir Square against [[Mohamed Morsi|Morsi]], 2012.
| image1            = Tahrir Square on February11.png
| image2            = TahrirSquareAgainstMorsi.jpg
}}

On 25 January 2011, [[2011  revolution|widespread protests]] erupted against President Hosni Mubarak's government, triggered by demands for political freedom, social justice, and the end of long-standing corruption. Demonstrations rapidly spread across major cities, particularly in [[Cairo]]'s [[Tahrir Square]], capturing global attention. After 18 days of escalating unrest, Mubarak resigned on 11 February 2011 and fled Cairo. The [[ military]] assumed control, with [[Mohamed Hussein Tantawi]], chairman of the [[Supreme Council of the Armed Forces]], acting as interim head of state. On 13 February, the military dissolved parliament and suspended the constitution. A constitutional referendum followed on 19 March 2011. Later that year,  held its first parliamentary elections since the previous regime, with high voter turnout and no major reported irregularities.

[[Mohamed Morsi]], affiliated with the [[Muslim Brotherhood]], won the [[2012  presidential election|presidency]] on 24 June 2012 and was sworn in on 30 June. His cabinet, announced in August 2012, included significant representation from the Muslim Brotherhood, prompting liberal and secular groups to withdraw from the [[Constituent Assembly of ]] over concerns of imposing strict Islamic law. In November 2012, Morsi issued a decree granting immunity to his decisions and protecting the constituent assembly's work, sparking [[2012–2013  protests|mass protests and violent clashes]] across the country. Tensions escalated, with the largest confrontations between Islamist supporters and opponents since the 2011 revolution occurring on 5 December 2012. Morsi refused to cancel the December 2012 [[2012  constitutional referendum|constitutional referendum]].

Following growing public discontent, the [[2013  coup d'état|military removed Morsi]] on 3 July 2013, dissolved the [[Senate ()|Shura Council]], and installed [[Adly Mansour]], chief justice of the [[Supreme Constitutional Court ()|Supreme Constitutional Court]], as interim president. Authorities cracked down on the Muslim Brotherhood, jailing thousands and conducting mass trials. Violence during [[Rabaa massacre|dispersals of pro-Morsi camps]] left hundreds dead. A new [[Constitution of |constitution]] was approved by referendum on 18 January 2014 with 98.1% voting in favour.

[[Abdel Fattah el-Sisi]] won the [[2014  presidential election|presidential elections]] in June 2014 with 96.1% of the vote and was sworn in on 8 June. Under his rule,  intensified security on the Gaza border, dismantled tunnels between Sinai and Gaza, and consolidated political power. Presidential terms were extended to six years in 2019, allowing El-Sisi to run for a third term in 2024. Parliamentary elections in 2020 confirmed a pro-El-Sisi majority for the [[Nation's Future Party|Mostaqbal Watan Party]]. The constitutional reforms and strengthened military authority under El-Sisi have been described as a return to authoritarianism.
== History ==
{{Main|History of }}

=== Prehistory and Aegean civilisations ===
{{Main|Neolithic |Pelasgians|Cycladic culture|Minoan civilisation|Mycenaean }}

[[File:Entrance to the treasure of Atreus.jpg|thumb|upright|The entrance of the [[Treasury of Atreus]] (13th century BC) in [[Mycenae]]|left]]

The [[Apidima Cave]] in [[Mani Peninsula|Mani]], in southern , has been suggested to contain the oldest remains of [[early modern humans]] outside of Africa, dated to 200,000 years ago. However others suggest the remains represent [[archaic humans]]. All three stages of the [[Stone Age]] are represented in , for example in the [[Franchthi Cave]]. [[Neolithic]] settlements in , dating from the 7th millennium BC, are the oldest in Europe, as  lies on the route by which farming spread from the [[Near East]] to Europe.

 is home to the first advanced civilisations in Europe and is often considered the birthplace of Western civilisation.{{Sfn|Duchesne|2011|p=297: "The list of books which have celebrated  as the "cradle" of the West is endless; two more examples are Charles Freeman's The  Achievement: The Foundation of the Western World (1999) and Bruce Thornton's  Ways: How the  Created Western Civilization (2000)"}}{{Sfn|Bottici|Challand|2013|p=88: "The reason why even such a sophisticated historian as Pagden can do it is that the idea that  is the cradle of civilisation is so much rooted in western minds and school curricula as to be taken for granted."}} The earliest of them was the [[Cycladic culture]] which flourished on the islands of the [[Aegean Sea]], starting around 3200 BC, and produced an abundance of folded-arm and other [[Cycladic art|marble figurines]]. From {{circa|3100}} BC to 1100 BC, [[Crete]], a major cultural and economic centre, was home to the [[Minoan civilization|Minoan civilisation]] known for its [[Minoan art|colourful art]], [[Minoan religion|religious figurines]], and [[Minoan palaces|monumental palaces]]. The Minoans wrote [[Minoan language|their undeciphered language]] using scripts known as [[Linear A]] and [[Cretan hieroglyphs]].{{Sfn|Tomas|2012|pp=340-351}}{{Sfn|Salgarella|2022}} On the mainland, the [[Mycenaean |Mycenaean civilisation]] developed around 1750 BC and lasted until {{circa|1100}} BC.{{Sfn|Knodell|2021|p=7}} The Mycenaeans possessed an [[Military of Mycenaean |advanced military]] and built [[Cyclopean masonry|large fortifications]]. They [[Mycenaean religion|worshiped]] [[List of Mycenaean deities|many gods]]{{Sfn|Neer|2019|pp=65-66}} and used [[Linear B]] to write the earliest [[Attested language|attested]] form of [[ language|]] known as [[Mycenaean ]].{{Sfn|Chadwick|1990}}

===Ancient ===
{{Main|Ancient }}

{{see also| Dark Ages|Archaic |Classical |Hellenistic }}
[[File: Colonization Archaic Period.svg|thumb|upright=1.2| territories and colonies during the [[Archaic |Archaic period]] (750–550 BC)]]
The collapse of the [[Mycenaean |Mycenaean civilisation]] ushered in the [[ Dark Ages]], from which written records are absent. The end of the Dark Ages is traditionally dated to 776 BC, the year of the first [[Ancient Olympic Games|Olympic Games]]. The ''[[Iliad]]'' and the ''[[Odyssey]]'', the foundational texts of [[Western literature]], are believed to have been composed by [[Homer]] in the 7th or 8th centuries BC. [[Ancient  literature|Poetry]] shaped beliefs to the [[ mythology|Olympian gods]], but [[ancient  religion]] had no priestly class or systematic dogmas and encompassed other currents, such as popular cults, like [[Cult of Dionysus|that of Dionysus]], [[Greco-Roman mysteries|mysteries]] and [[Magic in the Greco-Roman world|magic]]. At this time, many kingdoms and [[city-state]]s emerged across the  peninsula, some of which went on to establish [[ colonisation|a number of colonies]] in [[|Asia Minor]], the shores of the [[Black Sea]], and [[southern ]] (also known as [[Magna Grecia]]). The  city-states reached great prosperity that resulted in an unprecedented cultural boom, that of [[classical ]], expressed in [[Architecture of ancient |architecture]], [[Theatre of ancient |drama]], [[Ancient  science|science]], [[ mathematics|mathematics]] and [[Ancient  philosophy|philosophy]]. In 508 BC, [[Cleisthenes]] instituted the world's first [[Athenian democracy|democratic]] system of government in [[Athens]].

[[File:The Parthenon in Athens.jpg|thumb|left|The [[Parthenon]] on the [[Acropolis of Athens]], icon of classical ]]

By 500 BC, the [[Achaemenid Empire|Persian Empire]] controlled the  city states in Asia Minor and . Attempts by  city-states of Asia Minor to overthrow Persian rule [[Ionian Revolt|failed]], and Persia [[First Persian invasion of |invaded the states of mainland ]] in 492 BC, but was forced to withdraw after defeat at the [[Battle of Marathon]] in 490 BC. In response, the  city-states formed the Hellenic League in 481 BC, led by [[Sparta]], which was the first recorded union of  states since the mythical union of the [[Trojan War]]. The [[second Persian invasion of ]] was decisively defeated in 480&ndash;479 BC, at [[Battle of Salamis|Salamis]] and [[Battle of Plataea|Plataea]], marking the eventual withdrawal of the Persians from all their European territories. The  victories in the [[Greco-Persian Wars]] are a pivotal moment in history, as the 50 years of peace afterwards are known as the [[Fifth-century Athens|Golden Age of Athens]], a seminal period that laid many foundations of Western civilisation. Lack of political unity resulted in frequent conflict between  states. The most devastating intra- war was the [[Peloponnesian War]] (431–404 BC), which marked the demise of the [[Delian League|Athenian Empire]] and the emergence of [[Spartan hegemony|Spartan]] and later [[Theban hegemony]].{{Sfn|Worthington|2015|pp=42–43}} Weakened by constant wars among them during the 4th century BC, the  ''poleis'' were subjugated to the [[Rise of Macedon|rising power]] of the [[kingdom of Macedon]] under king [[Philip II of Macedon|Philip II]] into an alliance known as the [[League of Corinth|Hellenic League]].

{{multiple images
| image1            = Napoli BW 2013-05-16 16-24-01.jpg
| image2            = MacedonEmpire.jpg
| footer            = [[Alexander the Great]], whose conquests led to the [[Hellenistic period]]
| align             = right
| total_width       = 400
}}
After Philip's assassination in 336 BC, his son and [[king of Macedon]], [[Alexander the Great|Alexander]], established himself as leader of a [[Panhellenism|Panhellenic]] [[Wars of Alexander the Great|campaign]] against the [[Persian Empire]] and abolished it. Undefeated in battle, he marched, until his untimely death in 323 BC, to the banks of the [[Indus]]. Alexander's empire fragmented, inaugurating the [[Hellenistic period]]. After [[Wars of the Diadochi|fierce conflict]] amongst themselves, the [[Diadochi|generals that succeeded Alexander and their successors]] founded large personal kingdoms in the areas he had conquered, such as that of the [[Ptolemies]] in [[Ptolemaic Kingdom|]] and of the [[Seleucids]] in [[ (region)|]], [[Mesopotamia]] and [[ plateau|]]. The newly founded ''poleis'' of these kingdoms, such as [[Alexandria]] and [[Antioch]], were settled by  as members of a ruling minority. As a result, during the centuries that followed a vernacular form of [[Ancient  language|]], known as ''[[Koine |koine]]'', and  culture was [[Hellenization|spread]], while the  [[Hellenistic religion|adopted Eastern deities and cults]].  science, technology, and mathematics reached their peak during the Hellenistic period. Aspiring to maintain their autonomy and independence from the [[Antigonid dynasty|Antigonid kings]] of the [[Ancient |]], many ''poleis'' of  united in ''koina'' or ''[[sympoliteia]]i'' i.e. federations, while after the establishment of economic relations with the East, a stratum of wealthy ''[[Euergetism|euergetai]]'' dominated their internal life.

=== Roman province (146 BC – 4th century AD) ===
{{Main| in the Roman era}}

{{See also|Roman Empire}}
[[File:Athen Odeon Herodes Atticus BW 2017-10-09 13-12-44.jpg|thumb|upright=1.15|The [[Odeon of Herodes Atticus]] in Athens, built in 161 AD]]
In 280 BC [[Pyrrhus of Epirus|Pyrrhus]] of [[Epirus (ancient state)|Epirus]] campaigned in  against the Romans in order to help [[Taranto#History|Tarentum]]. From about 200 BC the [[Ancient Rome|Roman Republic]] became increasingly involved in  affairs and engaged in a [[ Wars|series of wars with Macedon]]. Macedon's defeat at the [[Battle of Pydna]] in 168 BC signalled the end of [[Antigonid dynasty|Antigonid]] power. In 146 BC,  was annexed as a province by Rome, and the rest of  became a Roman protectorate. The process was completed in 27 BC, when emperor [[Augustus]] annexed the rest of  and constituted it as the [[senatorial province]] of [[Achaea (Roman province)|Achaea]]. Despite their military superiority, the Romans admired and became [[Greco-Roman world|heavily influenced]] by  culture.

-speaking communities of the Hellenised East were instrumental in the spread of Christianity in the 2nd and 3rd centuries, and Christianity's early leaders and writers were mostly -speaking, though not from  itself. The [[New Testament]] was written in , and some sections attest to the importance of churches in  in [[early Christianity]]. Nevertheless, much of  clung to paganism, and ancient  religious practices were still in vogue in the late 4th century AD, when they were outlawed by the Roman emperor [[Theodosius I]] in 391–392. The last recorded Olympic games were held in 393, and many temples were destroyed or damaged in the century that followed. The closure of the [[Neoplatonism|Neoplatonic]] Academy of Athens by Emperor Justinian in 529 is considered the end of antiquity, although there is evidence that the academy continued.

=== Medieval period (4th–15th centuries) ===
{{Main|Byzantine |Frankokratia}}

[[File:Map Byzantine Empire 1025-en.svg|thumb|upright=1.25|The Byzantine (Eastern Roman) Empire after the death of [[Basil II]] in 1025]]
[[File:Καστροπολιτεια μονεμβασιας.jpg|thumb|upright=1|View of the medieval fortress city of [[Monemvasia]]]]
The Roman Empire in the east, following the [[fall of the Western Roman Empire]] in the 5th century, is known as the [[Byzantine Empire]], but called "Kingdom of the Romans" in its own time. With its capital in [[Constantinople]], its language and culture were  and its religion was predominantly [[Eastern Orthodox Church|Eastern Orthodox Christian]].

The Empire's Balkan territories, including , suffered from the dislocation of [[Migration Period|barbarian invasions]]; raids by [[Goths]] and [[Huns]] in the 4th and 5th centuries and the [[South Slavs|Slavic]] invasion in the 7th century resulted in a collapse in imperial authority in the  [[peninsula]].{{Sfn | Fine | 1991 | pp = 35–36}} The imperial government retained control of only the islands and coastal areas, particularly the populated walled cities such as Athens, Corinth and Thessalonica.{{Sfn | Fine | 1991 | pp = 35–36}}{{Sfn | Fine | 1991 | pp = 63–66}} However, the view that  underwent decline, fragmentation and depopulation is considered outdated, as cities show institutional continuity and prosperity between the 4th and 6th centuries. In the early 6th century,  had approximately 80 cities according to the [[Synecdemus|Synekdemos]] chronicle, and the 4th to the 7th century is considered one of high prosperity.

Until the 8th century almost all of modern  was under the jurisdiction of the [[Holy See]] of [[Rome]]. Byzantine [[Emperor Leo III]] moved the border of the [[Patriarchate of Constantinople]] westward and northward in the 8th century. The Byzantine recovery of lost provinces during the [[Arab–Byzantine wars]] began in the 8th century and most of the  peninsula came under imperial control again.{{Sfn|Fine|1991|pp=79–83}} This process was facilitated by a large influx of  from Sicily and Asia Minor to the  peninsula, while many Slavs were captured and re-settled in Asia Minor.{{Sfn|Fine|1991|pp=63–66}} During the 11th and 12th centuries the return of stability resulted in the  peninsula benefiting from economic growth. The [[ Orthodox Church]] was instrumental in the spread of  ideas to the wider [[Orthodoxy|Orthodox world]].

[[Sack of Constantinople|Constantinople was captured]] by the [[Fourth Crusade]] in 1204 becoming the capital of the [[Latin Empire]], which briefly ruled much of the former Byzantine lands. Three  rump states –the [[Despotate of Epirus]], the [[Empire of Nicaea]], and the [[Empire of Trebizond]]– were established by fleeing Byzantine aristocrats who rivaled the [[Frankokratia|Latin rule]]. The [[Reconquest of Constantinople|recapture of Constantinople]] as the imperial capital in 1261 by the Empire of Nicaea was accompanied by the empire's recovery of much of the  peninsula, while the islands remained under Genoese and Venetian control. During the [[Byzantine Empire under the Palaiologos dynasty|Paleologi dynasty]] (1261–1453) a new era of  patriotism emerged accompanied by a turning back to ancient . In the 14th century much of the  peninsula was lost by the Byzantine Empire to the [[Serbs]] and then the [[Ottoman Empire|Ottomans]]. [[Fall of Constantinople|Constantinople fell]] to the Ottomans in 1453 and by 1460, the Ottoman conquest of mainland  was complete.

=== Venetian possessions and Ottoman rule (15th century – 1821) ===
{{Main|Ottoman |Stato da Màr}}

{{See also|Kingdom of Candia|Venetian rule in the Ionian Islands}}
During the 15th to early 19th centuries, while much of [[Geography of |mainland ]] and the [[Aegean Islands|Aegean islands]] fell under [[Ottoman Empire|Ottoman]] control, [[Venice]] retained several key territories. Notably, [[Crete]] remained under [[Venetian rule in the Ionian Islands|Venetian rule]] until 1669, and the [[Ionian Islands]] were governed by Venice until 1797. These islands were subsequently ceded to [[First  Empire|]] and later [[ Empire|]] control.{{sfn|Clogg|1992|p=10}} While some  in the Ionian islands and [[Constantinople]] lived in prosperity, and  of Constantinople ([[Phanariots]]) achieved power within the Ottoman administration,{{sfn|Clogg|1992|p=23}} much of  suffered the economic consequences of Ottoman conquest. Heavy taxes were enforced, and in later years the Ottoman Empire enacted a policy of creation of hereditary estates, effectively turning the rural  populations into [[serfdom|serfs]], while the Ottoman conquest had cut  off from European historical developments.{{Sfn|Clogg|1992|pages=3}}

The [[ Orthodox Church]] and the [[Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople]] were considered by the Ottoman governments as the ruling authorities of the entire [[Eastern Orthodox Church|Orthodox Christian]] population of the Ottoman Empire, whether ethnically  or not. Although the Ottoman state did not force non-Muslims to convert to [[Islam]], Christians faced discrimination. Discrimination, particularly when combined with harsh treatment by local Ottoman authorities, led to conversions to Islam, if only superficially. In the 19th century, many "[[Crypto-Christianity|crypto-Christians]]" returned to their old religious allegiance.{{sfn|Clogg|1992|p=14}}

[[File:White Tower and Beach front.jpg|thumb|The [[White Tower of Thessaloniki]], one of the best-known Ottoman structures remaining in ]]

The nature of Ottoman administration of  varied, though it was invariably arbitrary and often harsh.{{Sfn|Clogg|1992|p=14}} Some cities had governors appointed by the [[Ottoman Sultan|Sultan]], while others, like Athens, were self-governed municipalities. Mountainous regions in the interior and many islands remained effectively autonomous from the central Ottoman state for centuries. The 16th and 17th centuries are regarded as a "dark age" in  history.{{Sfn|Clogg|1992|page=15}} However, prior to the  Revolution of 1821, there had been wars which saw  fight against the Ottomans, such as the  participation in the [[Battle of Lepanto]] in 1571,{{sfn|Clogg|1992|p=15}} the [[Morean War]] of 1684–1699, and the [[ Empire|]]-instigated [[Orlov revolt]] in 1770. These uprisings were put down by the Ottomans with great bloodshed. Many  were conscripted as Ottoman subjects to serve in the Ottoman army and especially the navy, while the Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople, responsible for the Orthodox, remained in general loyal to the Empire.

===Modern nation-state===
{{Main|History of modern }}

====  War of Independence (1821–1832) ====
{{Main| War of Independence}}

[[File:The sortie of Messologhi by Theodore Vryzakis.jpg|thumb|upright=.85|''The sortie (exodus) of [[Messolonghi]]'', depicting the [[third siege of Missolonghi]], painted by [[Theodoros Vryzakis]]]]
In the 18th century,  merchants came to dominate trade within the Ottoman Empire, established communities throughout the Mediterranean, the Balkans, and Europe,{{Sfn|Clogg|1992|pages=21, 23}} and used their wealth to fund educational activities that brought younger generations into contact with Western ideas.{{Sfn|Clogg|1992|page=25, 26, 27}} In the 18th century, an increase in learning during the [[Modern  Enlightenment]] led to the emergence among [[Westernization|Westernised]] -speaking [[elite]]s of the [[ nationalism|notion of a  nation]]. A secret organisation formed in this milieu was the [[Filiki Eteria]], in 1814. They engaged [[traditional society|traditional strata]] of the  Orthodox world in their [[liberal nationalism|liberal nationalist]] cause.

The first revolt began on 6 March 1821 in the [[Danubian Principalities]], but was put down by the Ottomans. This spurred the  of the [[Peloponnese]] and on 17 March the [[Maniots]] declared war on the Ottomans. By October 1821 the  had captured [[Tripoli, |Tripolitsa]]. There were revolts in Crete, [[ ()|]] and [[Central  (geographic region)|Central ]], which were suppressed. In 1822 and 1824 the Turks and  ravaged the islands, committing [[Massacres during the  Revolution|massacres]]. This galvanised opinion in western Europe in favour of the . The [[Ottoman Sultan]] [[Mahmud II]] negotiated with [[Mehmet Ali of ]], who agreed to send his son [[Ibrahim Pasha of |Ibrahim Pasha]] with an army, in return for territorial gain. By the end of 1825, most of the Peloponnese was under  control. Three [[great powers]], [[Bourbon Restoration in |]], [[ Empire]], and the [[]], each sent a navy. The allied fleet destroyed the Ottoman– fleet at the [[Battle of Navarino]], and the  captured Central  by 1828. The [[First Hellenic Republic|nascent  state]] was recognised under the [[London Protocol (1830)|London Protocol]] in 1830.

====Kingdom of ====
{{Main|Kingdom of }}

[[File:Peter von Hess - The Entry of King Othon of  in Athens - WGA11387.jpg|thumb|upright=1.1|left|''[[The Entry of King Otto of  into Athens]]'', painted by [[Peter von Hess]] in 1839]]

In 1827, [[Ioannis Kapodistrias]], was chosen by the [[Third National Assembly at Troezen]] as the first governor of the [[First Hellenic Republic]]. Kapodistrias established state, economic and military institutions. Tensions appeared between him and local interests and, following his assassination in 1831 and the [[London Conference of 1832]], Britain,  and  installed Bavarian Prince [[Otto of |Otto von Wittelsbach]] as [[Kingdom of  (Wittelsbach)|monarch]]. Otto's reign was [[despotism|despotic]], and in its first 11 years of independence  was ruled by a Bavarian oligarchy led by [[Josef Ludwig von Armansperg]] and, later, by Otto himself, as King and Premier.  remained under the influence of its three protecting great powers. In 1843 an [[3 September 1843 Revolution|uprising]] forced Otto to grant a [[ Constitution of 1844|constitution]] and [[Hellenic Parliament|representative assembly]].

Despite the [[absolute monarchy|absolutism]] of Otto's reign, it proved instrumental in developing institutions which are still the bedrock of  administration and education. Reforms were taken in education, maritime and postal communications, effective civil administration and the [[legal code]].{{sfn|Great  Encyclopedia|page=50–51}} [[Historical revisionism]] took the form of de-[[Byzantine Empire|Byzantinification]] and de-[[Ottoman Empire|Ottomanisation]], in favour of promoting Ancient  heritage.{{sfn|Roudometof|2001|pp=101–113}} The capital was moved from [[Nafplio]], where it had been since 1829, to [[Athens]], then a smaller town. The [[Church of ]] was established as 's [[national church]] and 25 March, the day of [[Annunciation]], was chosen as the anniversary of the [[ War of Independence]] to reinforce the link between  identity and [[Orthodoxy]].{{sfn|Roudometof|2001|pp=101–113}}

Otto [[23 October 1862 Revolution|was deposed]] in 1862 because of the Bavarian-dominated government, heavy taxation, and a failed attempt to annex Crete from the Ottomans. He was replaced by Prince Wilhelm of , who took the name [[George I of |George I]] and brought with him the Ionian Islands as a coronation gift from Britain. A [[ Constitution of 1864|new Constitution in 1864]] changed 's form of government from [[constitutional monarchy]] to the more democratic [[crowned republic]].{{sfn|Great  Encyclopedia|page=239|loc="Διὰ τοῦ Συντάγματος τοῦ 1864 καθιερώθει ὡς πολίτευμα διὰ τὴν Ἑλλάδα ἡ κοινοβουλευτικὴ μοναρχία, ἣ, ὅπως ἄλλως ἐχαρακτηρίσθη, ἡ «βασιλευομένη δημοκρατία» ἣ «δημοκρατικὴ βασιλεία»" [Through the Constitution of 1864, constitutional monarchy, or, as it had been described, "crowned democracy", or "democratic monarchy", was consolidated as the form of government in ]}} In 1875 [[parliamentary majority]] as a requirement for government was introduced, curbing the power of the monarchy to appoint [[minority government]]s. Corruption, coupled with increased spending to fund infrastructure like the [[Corinth Canal]], overtaxed the weak economy and forced the declaration of [[Sovereign default|public insolvency]] in 1893.

[[File:Map  expansion 1832-1947-en.svg|thumb|upright=1.15 |The territorial evolution of the [[Kingdom of ]] from 1832 to 1947]]

 were united, however, in their determination to liberate the [[regions of ancient |Hellenic lands]] under Ottoman rule; the [[Cretan Revolt (1866–1869)]] had raised nationalist fervour. When war broke out between [[Russo- War (1877–1878)| and the Ottomans in 1877]],  sentiment rallied to , but  was too poor and concerned about  intervention, to enter the war.  in Crete continued to stage revolts, and in 1897, the  government, bowing to popular pressure, declared war on the Ottomans. In the ensuing [[Greco- War (1897)|Greco- War of 1897]], the badly trained and equipped  army was defeated. Through the intervention of the Great Powers, however,  lost little territory, while Crete was established as an [[Cretan State|autonomous state]] under [[Prince George of ]]. With state coffers empty, fiscal policy came under [[International Financial Control]]. The government, aiming to quell [[Komitadjis]] and detach the [[Slavic speakers in Ottoman |Slavophone peasants of the region]] from [[ |]] influence, sponsored a [[guerrilla warfare|guerrilla]] campaign in Ottoman-ruled [[ (region)|]], known as the [[ Struggle]], which ended with the [[Young Turk Revolution]] in 1908.

==== Expansion, disaster, and reconstruction ====
{{See also| in the Balkan Wars| during World War I|National Schism|Second Hellenic Republic}}
Amidst dissatisfaction with the seeming inertia and unattainability of [[Megali Idea|national aspirations]], military officers organised a [[Goudi coup|coup]] in 1909 and called on [[Cretan State|Cretan]] politician [[Eleftherios Venizelos]], who conveyed a vision of national regeneration. After winning [[ legislative election, August 1910|two]] [[ legislative election, November 1910|elections]] and becoming prime minister in 1910,{{sfn|Mazower|1992|pp=886, 890–893, 895–900, 904}} Venizelos initiated fiscal, social, and [[ Constitution of 1911|constitutional reforms]], reorganised the military, made  a member of the [[Balkan League]], and led it through the [[Balkan Wars]]. By 1913, 's territory and population had doubled, annexing Crete, [[Epirus (region)|Epirus]], and [[ ()|]]. The struggle between [[Constantine I of |King Constantine I]] and charismatic Venizelos over foreign policy on the eve of the First World War dominated politics and divided the country into [[National Schism|two opposing groups]]. During parts of the war,  had two governments: A royalist [[Central Powers|pro-]] one in [[Athens]] and a [[Venizelism|Venizelist]] pro-[[Triple Entente|Entente]] one in [[Thessaloniki]]. They united in 1917, when  entered the war on the side of the Entente.

After the war,  attempted expansion into [[Asia Minor]], a region with a large native  population, but was defeated in the [[Greco- War (1919–1922)]], contributing to a flight of [[Ottoman |Asia Minor ]]. These events overlapped, happening during the [[ genocide]] (1914–22), when Ottoman and  officials contributed to the death of several hundred thousand Asia Minor , along with similar numbers of [[Assyrian genocide|Assyrians]] and a larger number of [[ genocide|]]. The resultant  exodus from Asia Minor was made permanent, and expanded, in an official [[population exchange between  and ]], as part of the [[Treaty of Lausanne]] which ended the war. The following era was marked by instability, as over 1.5&nbsp;million propertyless  refugees from  (some of whom could not speak ) had to be integrated into  society. The refugees made a dramatic population boost, as they were more than a quarter of 's prior population.

Following the catastrophic events in Asia Minor, the monarchy was abolished [[ republic referendum, 1924|via a referendum]] in 1924 and the [[Second Hellenic Republic]] declared. In 1935, a royalist general-turned-politician [[Georgios Kondylis]] took power after a coup and abolished the republic, holding [[ monarchy referendum, 1935|a rigged referendum]], after which [[George II of |King George II]] was restored to the throne.

==== Dictatorship, World War II, and reconstruction ====
{{See also|4th of August Regime|Balkans campaign (World War II){{!}}Balkans campaign|Axis occupation of |Hellenic State (1941–1944){{!}}Hellenic State| Civil War}}

An agreement between Prime Minister [[Ioannis Metaxas]] and George II followed in 1936, which installed Metaxas as head of a dictatorship known as the [[4th of August Regime]], inaugurating [[authoritarianism|authoritarian rule]] that would last until 1974.  remained on good terms with Britain and was not allied with the [[Axis powers|Axis]].

[[File:Αθηναίοι γιορτάζουν την απελευθέρωση της πόλης τους, Οκτώβριος 1944.jpg|thumb|right|People in [[Athens]] celebrate the liberation from the Axis powers, October 1944. Postwar  would soon experience a [[ civil war|civil war]] and political polarisation]]

In October 1940, [[Kingdom of |Fascist ]] demanded the surrender of , but it [[Ohi Day|refused]], and, in the [[Greco- War]],  repelled  forces into .{{sfn|Fafalios|Hadjipateras|1995|p=157}}  general [[Charles de Gaulle]] praised the fierceness of the  resistance, but the country fell to urgently dispatched [[Nazi |]] forces during the [[Battle of ]]. The Nazis proceeded to administer Athens and Thessaloniki, while other regions were given to Fascist  and . Over 100,000 civilians died of starvation during the winter of 1941–42, tens of thousands more died because of reprisals by Nazis and [[Collaboration with Nazi  and Fascist #|collaborators]], the economy was ruined, and most [[History of the Jews in | Jews]] (tens of thousands) were deported and murdered in Nazi concentration camps.{{sfn|Mazower|2001|p=155}} The [[ Resistance]], one of the most effective resistance movements, fought against the Nazis. The  occupiers committed [[ war crimes#|atrocities, mass executions, and wholesale slaughter of civilians and destruction of towns and villages]] in reprisals. Hundreds of villages were systematically torched and almost 1 million  left homeless.{{sfn|Mazower|2001|p=155}} The  executed around 21,000 , the  40,000, and the  9,000.<!-- Does this include the 60,000-70,000 Jews mentioned at [[History_of_the_Jews_in_Greece#World_War_II,_Resistance_and_the_Holocaust]] ?-->

Following liberation,  annexed the [[Dodecanese Islands]] from  and regained [[Western Thrace]] from . The country descended into a [[ Civil War|civil war]] between [[Communism|communist]] forces and the anti-communist  government, which lasted until 1949, with the latter's victory. The conflict, one of the earliest struggles of the [[Cold War]], resulted in further economic devastation, population displacement and political polarisation for the next thirty years.{{sfn|Mazower|2016|p={{page needed|date=September 2024}}}}

Although post-war was characterised by social strife and marginalisation of the left,  experienced [[ economic miracle|rapid economic growth]] and recovery, propelled in part by the U.S. [[Marshall Plan]]. In 1952,  joined [[NATO]], reinforcing its membership in the [[Western Bloc]] of the Cold War.

[[Constantine II of |King Constantine II]]'s quick acceptance of [[George Papandreou (senior)|George Papandreou]]'s informal resignation as prime minister in 1965 prompted an era of political turbulence that was later called [[Iouliana]], and culminated in a coup in 1967 by the [[ junta]], led by [[Georgios Papadopoulos]]. Civil rights were suspended, political repression intensified, and human rights abuses, including torture, were rampant. Economic growth remained rapid before plateauing in 1972. The brutal suppression of the [[Athens Polytechnic uprising]] in 1973 set in motion the fall of the regime, resulting in a counter-coup that established brigadier [[Dimitrios Ioannidis]] as the new junta strongman. On 20 July 1974, [[ invasion of | invaded the island of ]] in response to a -backed  coup, triggering a crisis in  that led to the regime's collapse and restoration of democracy through [[Metapolitefsi]].

==== Third Hellenic Republic ====
{{Main|Third Hellenic Republic}}

[[File:Accession of  to the European Union.png|thumb|Signing at [[Zappeion]] by [[Constantine Karamanlis]] of the documents for the accession of  to the [[European Union|European Communities]] in 1979]]

The former prime minister [[Konstantinos Karamanlis]] was invited back from self-exile and the [[ legislative election, 1974|first multiparty elections]] since 1964 were held on the first anniversary of the Polytechnic uprising. A democratic and republican [[Constitution of |constitution]] was promulgated in 1975 following a [[ republic referendum, 1974|referendum]] which chose not to restore the monarchy.

Meanwhile, [[Andreas Papandreou]], George Papandreou's son, founded the [[Panhellenic Socialist Movement]] (PASOK) in response to Karamanlis's conservative [[New Democracy ()|New Democracy]] party, with the two political formations dominating government over the next four decades.  rejoined NATO in 1980.{{Refn | group = lower-alpha | name="integrated1974" | On 14 August 1974  forces withdrew from the integrated military structure of [[NATO]] in protest at the  occupation of northern ;  rejoined NATO in 1980.}}  became the tenth member of the [[European Communities]] in 1981, ushering in sustained growth. Investments in industrial enterprises and heavy infrastructure, as well as funds from the [[European Union]] and growing revenue from tourism, shipping, and a fast-growing service sector raised the [[standard of living]]. In 1981, [[Andreas Papandreou]] came to power and implemented an [[Social reforms of Andreas Papandreou|ambitious program of social reforms]]. He recognised civil marriage, the dowry was abolished, while expanding access to education and health care. However, he made controversial [[Foreign policy of Andreas Papandreou|foreign policy]] decisions that fueled the rise of [[terrorism in ]]. Papandreou's tenure has been associated with corruption (see [[Koskotas scandal|Koskotas]] and [[Yugoslav corn scandal]]s) and the [[1985  constitutional crisis|first constitutional crisis of the new republic]], while [[Economic policies of Andreas Papandreou|his economic policies]] failed to address the persistent [[stagflation]] and chronic budget deficits that exacerbated 's economic problems.

The country adopted the euro in 2001 and successfully hosted the [[2004 Summer Olympics|2004 Summer Olympic Games]] in Athens. In 2010,  suffered from the [[Great Recession]] and related [[European sovereign debt crisis]]. Due to the adoption of the euro,  could no longer [[Devaluation|devalue]] its currency to regain competitiveness. In the 2012 elections, there was major political change, with new parties emerging from the collapse of the two main parties, PASOK and New Democracy. In 2015, [[Alexis Tsipras]] was elected as prime minister, the first outside the two main parties. The [[ government-debt crisis]], and subsequent austerity policies, resulted in social strife. The crisis ended around 2018, with the end of the bailout mechanisms and return of growth. Simultaneously, Tsipras, and the leader of , [[Zoran Zaev]], signed the [[Prespa Agreement]], solving the [[ naming dispute|naming dispute]] that had strained the relations and eased the latter's way to become a member of the EU and NATO.

In 2019, [[Kyriakos Mitsotakis]] became 's new prime minister, after his centre-right New Democracy won the [[2019  legislative election|election]]. In 2020, 's parliament elected a non-partisan candidate, [[Katerina Sakellaropoulou]], as the first female [[President of ]]. In February 2024,  became the first Orthodox Christian country to recognise same-sex marriage and adoption by same-sex couples.

In 2023,  became a member of the Three Seas Initiative.
